## Chapter 3: Ocean Acoustics:




### Introduction

In the vast and mysterious world of the ocean, sound plays a crucial role in communication, navigation, and understanding the environment. The study of underwater sound propagation is a fascinating and complex field known as acoustical oceanography. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of underwater sound propagation, exploring the depths of its properties, behavior, and impact on marine life.

Underwater sound propagation is a multifaceted topic, encompassing a wide range of phenomena from the transmission of sound waves through water to the interaction of these waves with the ocean floor, surface, and marine life. It is a field that is constantly evolving, with new research and technologies continually expanding our understanding of this fascinating subject.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of underwater sound propagation, including the behavior of sound waves in water, the effects of water properties on sound transmission, and the role of sound in marine ecosystems. We will also discuss the practical applications of this knowledge, such as in underwater communication and navigation systems, and the challenges and opportunities presented by the unique properties of underwater sound.

Whether you are a seasoned researcher, a student new to the field, or simply a curious reader, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive and accessible introduction to the fascinating world of underwater sound propagation. So, let's dive in and explore the depths of sound in the ocean.




#### 3.1a Specular Reflection

Specular reflection is a fundamental concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the reflection of sound waves from a surface, where the angle of incidence is equal to the angle of reflection. This phenomenon is governed by the law of reflection, which states that the angle of incidence is equal to the angle of reflection.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, specular reflection plays a crucial role in determining the direction of sound waves. It is particularly important in the study of echoes, where sound waves are reflected back from objects in the water. The law of reflection allows us to predict the direction of these echoes, which is essential for applications such as sonar and acoustical imaging.

The law of reflection can be mathematically expressed as:

$$
\theta_i = \theta_r
$$

where $\theta_i$ is the angle of incidence and $\theta_r$ is the angle of reflection, both measured from the normal to the surface.

In the case of a smooth, flat surface, the law of reflection can be extended to the concept of specular reflection. This is where the sound waves are reflected back in the same direction from which they came, resulting in a mirror-like reflection. The law of reflection can be extended to specular reflection as follows:

$$
\theta_i = \theta_r = \theta
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle of incidence and reflection, both measured from the normal to the surface.

However, in the ocean, surfaces are often not smooth and flat. Instead, they are rough and irregular, leading to diffuse reflection. This is where the sound waves are scattered in different directions, resulting in a more complex reflection pattern. The study of diffuse reflection is a topic of ongoing research in acoustical oceanography.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of diffuse reflection and explore its implications for underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1b Diffuse Reflection

Diffuse reflection is a phenomenon that occurs when sound waves are scattered in different directions due to the roughness of a surface. Unlike specular reflection, where sound waves are reflected back in the same direction from which they came, diffuse reflection results in a more complex reflection pattern. This is because the roughness of the surface causes the sound waves to scatter in different directions, leading to a diffuse reflection.

The mathematical description of diffuse reflection is more complex than that of specular reflection. The law of reflection, which states that the angle of incidence is equal to the angle of reflection, cannot be applied directly to diffuse reflection. Instead, we need to consider the concept of the reflection coefficient, which is a measure of the proportion of sound waves that are reflected from a surface.

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $R(\theta_i)$, is a function of the angle of incidence $\theta_i$. It is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. For a rough surface, the reflection coefficient is a function of the angle of incidence and the roughness of the surface.

The reflection coefficient can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
R(\theta_i) = |r(\theta_i)|^2
$$

where $r(\theta_i)$ is the reflection coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$. The absolute value squared operation in the equation represents the power of the reflected wave.

The reflection coefficient is a crucial concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It allows us to predict the amount of sound that will be reflected from a surface, which is essential for applications such as sonar and acoustical imaging.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of absorption, another important aspect of underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1c Reflection Coefficient

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $R(\theta_i)$, is a fundamental concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It is a measure of the proportion of sound waves that are reflected from a surface, and it is a function of the angle of incidence $\theta_i$ and the roughness of the surface.

The reflection coefficient is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
R(\theta_i) = \frac{|r(\theta_i)|^2}{|t(\theta_i)|^2 + |r(\theta_i)|^2}
$$

where $r(\theta_i)$ is the reflection coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$, and $t(\theta_i)$ is the transmission coefficient for the same incident wave. The transmission coefficient represents the proportion of sound waves that are transmitted through the surface.

The reflection coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude represents the power of the reflected wave. The phase of the reflection coefficient represents the phase shift of the reflected wave relative to the incident wave.

The reflection coefficient is a crucial concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It allows us to predict the amount of sound that will be reflected from a surface, which is essential for applications such as sonar and acoustical imaging.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of absorption, another important aspect of underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1d Reflection Loss

Reflection loss is a critical concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It is a measure of the loss of sound energy due to reflection from a surface. The reflection loss is a function of the reflection coefficient and the absorption coefficient of the surface.

The reflection loss, denoted by $L_r(\theta_i)$, is defined as:

$$
L_r(\theta_i) = 10 \log_{10} \left( \frac{1}{R(\theta_i)} \right)
$$

where $R(\theta_i)$ is the reflection coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$. The reflection loss is expressed in decibels (dB), and it represents the power loss of the reflected wave relative to the incident wave.

The absorption coefficient, denoted by $A(\theta_i)$, is a measure of the proportion of sound energy that is absorbed by a surface. It is defined as the ratio of the absorbed wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
A(\theta_i) = 1 - R(\theta_i) - T(\theta_i)
$$

where $T(\theta_i)$ is the transmission coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$. The transmission coefficient represents the proportion of sound waves that are transmitted through the surface.

The absorption coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude represents the power of the absorbed wave relative to the incident wave. The phase of the absorption coefficient represents the phase shift of the absorbed wave relative to the incident wave.

The reflection loss is a crucial concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It allows us to predict the amount of sound energy that will be lost due to reflection from a surface, which is essential for applications such as sonar and acoustical imaging.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of absorption, another important aspect of underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1e Reflection Measurements

Reflection measurements are a crucial part of studying underwater sound propagation. These measurements provide direct information about the reflection loss, which is a key parameter in understanding how sound behaves when it encounters a surface.

Reflection measurements can be performed using a variety of techniques, including the use of hydrophones and acoustic cameras. Hydrophones are underwater microphones that can detect and measure the sound pressure of a reflected wave. Acoustic cameras, on the other hand, use an array of hydrophones to create a two-dimensional image of the reflected sound field.

The reflection loss can be calculated from these measurements using the formula:

$$
L_r(\theta_i) = 10 \log_{10} \left( \frac{1}{R(\theta_i)} \right)
$$

where $R(\theta_i)$ is the reflection coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$.

Reflection measurements can be challenging due to the complex nature of underwater sound propagation. The reflection loss can be affected by a variety of factors, including the roughness of the surface, the frequency of the sound, and the presence of bubbles or other particles in the water.

Despite these challenges, reflection measurements are an essential tool in the study of underwater sound propagation. They provide direct information about the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a surface, which can be used to validate theoretical models and to design more effective sonar systems.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of absorption, another important aspect of underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1f Reflection Modeling

Reflection modeling is a critical aspect of understanding underwater sound propagation. It involves the use of mathematical models to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a surface. These models can be used to calculate the reflection loss, which is a key parameter in understanding how sound behaves when it encounters a surface.

There are several types of reflection models, each with its own assumptions and limitations. One of the simplest models is the specular reflection model, which assumes that the sound waves are reflected in a single direction, determined by the angle of incidence. This model is often used for smooth surfaces, where the angle of incidence is equal to the angle of reflection.

The specular reflection model can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
R(\theta_i) = \left| \frac{1 - \cos(\theta_i)}{1 + \cos(\theta_i)} \right|^2
$$

where $R(\theta_i)$ is the reflection coefficient for an incident wave with angle of incidence $\theta_i$.

Another common model is the diffuse reflection model, which assumes that the sound waves are reflected in all directions. This model is often used for rough surfaces, where the angle of incidence is not necessarily equal to the angle of reflection.

The diffuse reflection model can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
R(\theta_i) = \frac{1 - \cos(\theta_i)}{2}
$$

These models can be used to calculate the reflection loss, as shown in the previous section. However, they are simplifications of the real-world behavior of sound waves, and their accuracy depends on the specific characteristics of the surface and the sound wave.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of absorption, another important aspect of underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.1g Reflection Applications

Reflection applications in underwater sound propagation are numerous and varied. They range from basic research to practical applications in various fields. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on their relevance and potential impact.

##### Sonar Systems

One of the most common applications of reflection modeling is in the design and operation of sonar systems. Sonar, short for sound navigation and ranging, is a technique that uses sound waves to navigate, communicate, and detect objects underwater. The accuracy of sonar systems depends on the ability to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a surface. By using reflection models, engineers can design sonar systems that can accurately detect and locate objects underwater.

##### Underwater Imaging

Reflection measurements and modeling are also used in underwater imaging. Acoustic cameras, for instance, use an array of hydrophones to create a two-dimensional image of the reflected sound field. This technique is particularly useful in underwater environments, where traditional imaging techniques may not be feasible. By using reflection models, researchers can interpret these images and extract useful information about the underwater environment.

##### Environmental Monitoring

Reflection measurements and modeling are also used in environmental monitoring. For example, they can be used to study the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can provide valuable insights into the ocean's physical properties and the presence of marine life. By using reflection models, researchers can predict the behavior of sound waves in different oceanic conditions, which can help in the design of more effective monitoring systems.

##### Underwater Communication

Reflection modeling is also used in underwater communication. By understanding how sound waves behave when they encounter a surface, researchers can design communication systems that can transmit and receive information reliably underwater. This is particularly important in applications such as underwater exploration and deep-sea communication.

In conclusion, reflection applications in underwater sound propagation are vast and varied. They range from basic research to practical applications in various fields. By using reflection models, researchers can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of sound waves in the underwater environment, which can lead to the development of more effective technologies and systems.

#### 3.1h Reflection Case Studies

In this section, we will delve into some specific case studies that illustrate the application of reflection modeling in underwater sound propagation. These case studies will provide a more concrete understanding of the concepts discussed in the previous sections.

##### Case Study 1: Sonar System Design

Consider the design of a sonar system for a submarine. The sonar system needs to accurately detect and locate objects underwater, such as other submarines or underwater mines. The sonar system uses sound waves to navigate and communicate, and its accuracy depends on the ability to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a surface.

By using reflection models, engineers can design a sonar system that can accurately detect and locate objects underwater. For instance, the specular reflection model can be used to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a smooth surface, such as the hull of another submarine. On the other hand, the diffuse reflection model can be used to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a rough surface, such as an underwater mine.

##### Case Study 2: Underwater Imaging

Consider the use of acoustic cameras in underwater imaging. An acoustic camera uses an array of hydrophones to create a two-dimensional image of the reflected sound field. This technique is particularly useful in underwater environments, where traditional imaging techniques may not be feasible.

By using reflection models, researchers can interpret these images and extract useful information about the underwater environment. For instance, the specular reflection model can be used to interpret the image when the sound waves are reflected in a single direction, such as off a smooth surface. On the other hand, the diffuse reflection model can be used to interpret the image when the sound waves are reflected in all directions, such as off a rough surface.

##### Case Study 3: Environmental Monitoring

Consider the use of reflection measurements and modeling in environmental monitoring. By studying the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, researchers can gain valuable insights into the ocean's physical properties and the presence of marine life.

By using reflection models, researchers can predict the behavior of sound waves in different oceanic conditions. For instance, the specular reflection model can be used to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a smooth surface, such as the surface of the ocean. On the other hand, the diffuse reflection model can be used to predict the behavior of sound waves when they encounter a rough surface, such as the surface of a marine organism.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the power and versatility of reflection modeling in underwater sound propagation. By using reflection models, researchers can design more effective sonar systems, interpret underwater images, and monitor the ocean's environment.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of underwater sound propagation. We have explored the unique characteristics of sound in water, the factors that influence its behavior, and the mathematical models that describe its propagation. We have also examined the practical applications of this knowledge in various fields, including marine biology, oceanography, and underwater acoustics.

We have learned that sound in water behaves differently from sound in air, due to the higher density and lower speed of sound in water. This leads to phenomena such as refraction and reflection, which can significantly alter the path and intensity of sound waves. We have also seen how the frequency of sound can affect its propagation, with higher frequencies experiencing greater absorption and scattering.

We have also discussed the mathematical models used to describe sound propagation in water, including the wave equation and the ray theory. These models provide a quantitative understanding of sound propagation, allowing us to predict the behavior of sound waves in different conditions.

Finally, we have explored some of the practical applications of this knowledge. For example, we have seen how understanding sound propagation can help us design more effective sonar systems, or study the behavior of marine animals.

In conclusion, underwater sound propagation is a complex and fascinating field, with many practical applications. By understanding the principles and mathematical models involved, we can gain a deeper understanding of the underwater world, and develop more effective technologies for exploring and studying it.

## Chapter 4: Bottom Loss

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex environment, and understanding its acoustics is a challenging but crucial task. In this chapter, we delve into the concept of bottom loss, a fundamental aspect of underwater sound propagation. Bottom loss, also known as seafloor loss, is a phenomenon that occurs when sound waves encounter the ocean floor. It is a critical factor in determining the range and clarity of underwater communication and navigation systems.

Bottom loss is a result of the interaction between sound waves and the seafloor. The seafloor, like the air above it, has a speed of sound that is different from that of water. This difference in speed leads to phenomena such as reflection, refraction, and absorption, which collectively contribute to bottom loss. The magnitude of bottom loss depends on several factors, including the frequency of the sound, the type of seafloor (hard or soft), and the depth of the water.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe bottom loss. These models, expressed in terms of the frequency of the sound and the properties of the seafloor, provide a quantitative understanding of bottom loss. We will also discuss the practical implications of bottom loss, including its impact on underwater communication and navigation systems.

Understanding bottom loss is not just about understanding the interaction between sound waves and the seafloor. It is also about understanding the broader context of underwater acoustics. Bottom loss is one of the key factors that determine the propagation of sound in the ocean. By understanding bottom loss, we can better understand the behavior of sound in the ocean, and develop more effective technologies for underwater communication and navigation.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the topic of bottom loss, exploring its mathematical description, its practical implications, and its role in the broader context of underwater acoustics. We will also discuss some of the latest research in this field, providing a glimpse into the cutting-edge of knowledge in this exciting area of study.




#### 3.1b Rough Surface Reflection

Rough surface reflection is a complex phenomenon that occurs when sound waves encounter a surface that is not smooth and flat. Unlike specular reflection, where sound waves are reflected back in the same direction from which they came, rough surface reflection results in a scattering of sound waves in different directions. This scattering is due to the irregularities and variations in the surface, which cause the sound waves to change direction and intensity as they interact with the surface.

The study of rough surface reflection is crucial in acoustical oceanography, as it helps us understand how sound waves interact with the ocean floor, the seafloor topography, and the surfaces of objects submerged in the water. This understanding is essential for applications such as sonar, acoustical imaging, and underwater communication.

The mathematical modeling of rough surface reflection is a challenging task due to the complexity of the phenomenon. However, several models have been developed to describe this process. One of the most commonly used models is the Lambertian reflection model, which assumes that the surface is perfectly diffuse and that the reflected sound waves are uniformly distributed in all directions. This model is often used in the study of rough surface reflection, but it is not always accurate, especially for surfaces with strong directional properties.

Another model that is often used is the specular-diffuse reflection model, which combines the Lambertian reflection model with a specular reflection component. This model is particularly useful for surfaces with both smooth and rough areas, where some sound waves are reflected specularly and others are reflected diffusely.

The study of rough surface reflection is an active area of research in acoustical oceanography. Researchers are constantly developing new models and techniques to better understand and predict this complex phenomenon. As our understanding of rough surface reflection improves, so too will our ability to predict and control the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.1c Reflection Measurements

Reflection measurements are a crucial part of studying underwater sound propagation. These measurements provide direct information about the interaction of sound waves with the ocean floor, seafloor topography, and submerged objects. They are essential for understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean and for developing accurate models of sound propagation.

There are several methods for measuring reflection, including the use of hydrophones, acoustic imaging, and acoustic tomography. Each of these methods has its advantages and limitations, and they are often used in combination to provide a comprehensive understanding of the reflection process.

Hydrophones are underwater microphones that can detect and measure the intensity of sound waves. They are often used to measure the reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor or from submerged objects. Hydrophones can provide detailed information about the direction and intensity of the reflected sound waves, but they are limited in their ability to provide information about the surface properties of the reflecting surface.

Acoustic imaging is another method for measuring reflection. It involves the use of acoustic sources and receivers to create an image of the reflecting surface. This method can provide information about the surface properties of the reflecting surface, such as its roughness and directional properties, but it is limited in its ability to provide information about the intensity of the reflected sound waves.

Acoustic tomography is a method for measuring the reflection of sound waves over a large area. It involves the use of multiple acoustic sources and receivers to create a tomographic image of the reflecting surface. This method can provide a comprehensive view of the reflection process, but it is limited in its ability to provide detailed information about the surface properties of the reflecting surface.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of diffuse reflection and its importance in acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.1d Reflection Applications

Reflection measurements have a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. They are used to study the interaction of sound waves with the ocean floor, seafloor topography, and submerged objects. This information is crucial for understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean and for developing accurate models of sound propagation.

One of the primary applications of reflection measurements is in the study of the ocean floor. The reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor can provide valuable information about the surface properties of the ocean floor, such as its roughness and directional properties. This information can be used to create detailed maps of the ocean floor, which can be used for navigation, resource exploration, and environmental monitoring.

Reflection measurements are also used in the study of seafloor topography. The reflection of sound waves from the seafloor can provide information about the shape and structure of the seafloor. This information can be used to create detailed maps of the seafloor, which can be used for navigation, resource exploration, and environmental monitoring.

Another important application of reflection measurements is in the study of submerged objects. The reflection of sound waves from submerged objects can provide information about the surface properties of the objects, such as their size, shape, and material properties. This information can be used to identify and track submerged objects, which can be useful for navigation, resource exploration, and environmental monitoring.

Reflection measurements are also used in the development of acoustic models of sound propagation in the ocean. These models are used to predict the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which is crucial for applications such as sonar, acoustic imaging, and underwater communication.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of diffuse reflection and its importance in acoustical oceanography.




#### 3.1c Bottom Reflection

Bottom reflection is a critical aspect of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor or the seafloor topography. This phenomenon is particularly important in acoustical oceanography, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves in the water column.

The reflection of sound waves from the bottom is influenced by several factors, including the properties of the ocean floor, the frequency of the sound waves, and the angle of incidence. The ocean floor can be classified into two main types: hard and soft. Hard surfaces, such as rock or sand, tend to reflect sound waves more strongly than soft surfaces, such as mud or sediment.

The frequency of the sound waves also plays a crucial role in bottom reflection. Lower frequencies, typically below 1 kHz, tend to penetrate deeper into the water column and are less affected by the bottom. However, higher frequencies, above 1 kHz, are more likely to be reflected by the bottom.

The angle of incidence, or the angle at which the sound waves hit the bottom, is another important factor. When the angle of incidence is small, the sound waves are more likely to be reflected back into the water column. As the angle of incidence increases, the likelihood of the sound waves being transmitted into the bottom increases.

The mathematical modeling of bottom reflection is a complex task due to the interplay of these factors. However, several models have been developed to describe this process. One of the most commonly used models is the bottom reflection coefficient, which is defined as the ratio of the reflected sound wave amplitude to the incident sound wave amplitude. This coefficient is a function of the bottom properties, the frequency of the sound waves, and the angle of incidence.

Another important model is the bottom bounce loss, which is the loss of sound energy due to reflection from the bottom. This loss is typically expressed in decibels (dB) and is a function of the bottom properties and the frequency of the sound waves.

The study of bottom reflection is crucial in acoustical oceanography, as it helps us understand how sound waves interact with the ocean floor and the seafloor topography. This understanding is essential for applications such as sonar, acoustical imaging, and underwater communication.




#### 3.2a Snell's Law and Sound Speed Gradients

Snell's Law is a fundamental principle in acoustics that describes how sound waves are refracted when they pass from one medium to another. In the context of underwater sound propagation, Snell's Law is particularly important as it helps us understand how sound waves are affected by changes in the properties of the water.

Snell's Law can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
n_1 \sin(\theta_1) = n_2 \sin(\theta_2)
$$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the refractive indices of the two media, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively. The refractive index is a measure of how much a medium slows down the speed of sound. In water, the refractive index is typically around 1.33, but it can vary depending on the temperature, salinity, and pressure of the water.

Sound speed gradients, on the other hand, refer to the changes in the speed of sound with respect to changes in the properties of the water. These gradients can be caused by changes in temperature, salinity, or pressure. For example, as we move deeper into the water column, the pressure increases, which can cause the speed of sound to increase. Similarly, as the temperature or salinity of the water changes, the speed of sound can also change.

The effect of sound speed gradients on underwater sound propagation is significant. As the speed of sound changes, the wavelength of the sound waves also changes. This can lead to changes in the direction of propagation, as well as changes in the intensity of the sound. In addition, sound speed gradients can also cause refraction, as the speed of sound changes across the boundary between two media.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles, which are graphical representations of the changes in sound speed with depth in the water column. These profiles can provide valuable insights into the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.2b Refraction and Bottom Bounce

Refraction and bottom bounce are two key processes that affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. As we have seen in the previous section, refraction occurs when sound waves pass from one medium to another, and it is governed by Snell's Law. Bottom bounce, on the other hand, refers to the reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor.

The bottom bounce is a crucial aspect of underwater sound propagation, particularly in the context of acoustical oceanography. It is the process by which sound waves are reflected from the ocean floor, and it plays a significant role in the propagation of sound waves in the water column.

The bottom bounce can be modeled using the concept of the bottom reflection coefficient, which is defined as the ratio of the reflected sound wave amplitude to the incident sound wave amplitude. This coefficient is a function of the properties of the ocean floor, including its hardness and roughness, as well as the frequency and angle of incidence of the sound waves.

The bottom reflection coefficient can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
R = \frac{A_{reflected}}{A_{incident}} = \frac{Z_{water} - Z_{bottom}}{Z_{water} + Z_{bottom}}
$$

where $R$ is the bottom reflection coefficient, $A_{reflected}$ and $A_{incident}$ are the amplitudes of the reflected and incident sound waves, respectively, $Z_{water}$ and $Z_{bottom}$ are the impedances of the water and the bottom, respectively.

The bottom reflection coefficient can range from 0 (no reflection) to 1 (complete reflection). A higher bottom reflection coefficient indicates a harder and smoother ocean floor, which leads to stronger bottom bounce.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles, which are graphical representations of the changes in sound speed with depth in the water column. These profiles can provide valuable insights into the propagation of sound waves in the ocean, including the effects of refraction and bottom bounce.

#### 3.2c Refraction and Bottom Bounce in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the effects of refraction and bottom bounce are particularly pronounced due to the relatively short water column. The shallow water environment presents unique challenges and opportunities for acoustical oceanography. 

Refraction in shallow water is governed by the same principles as in deep water, but the effects are more pronounced due to the shorter water column. The speed of sound in water increases with depth, which means that the wavelength of sound waves decreases with depth. This can lead to significant changes in the direction of propagation, as well as changes in the intensity of the sound.

The bottom bounce in shallow water is also a critical aspect of sound propagation. The reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor can significantly alter the propagation path of the sound waves. This is particularly important in shallow water, where the water column is often much shorter than the wavelength of the sound.

The bottom reflection coefficient in shallow water can be modeled using the same equation as in deep water:

$$
R = \frac{A_{reflected}}{A_{incident}} = \frac{Z_{water} - Z_{bottom}}{Z_{water} + Z_{bottom}}
$$

However, in shallow water, the effects of bottom bounce can be more pronounced due to the shorter water column. This means that the bottom reflection coefficient can have a larger impact on the propagation of sound waves in shallow water.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles, which are graphical representations of the changes in sound speed with depth in the water column. These profiles can provide valuable insights into the propagation of sound waves in the ocean, including the effects of refraction and bottom bounce.

#### 3.2d Refraction and Bottom Bounce in Deep Water

In deep water, the effects of refraction and bottom bounce are more subtle but still crucial to understanding sound propagation in the ocean. The longer water column in deep water allows for more complex interactions between sound waves and the ocean floor.

Refraction in deep water is governed by the same principles as in shallow water, but the effects are more subtle due to the longer water column. The speed of sound in water increases with depth, which means that the wavelength of sound waves decreases with depth. However, in deep water, this change in wavelength is often much smaller than in shallow water, leading to less pronounced changes in the direction of propagation.

The bottom bounce in deep water is also a critical aspect of sound propagation. The reflection of sound waves from the ocean floor can significantly alter the propagation path of the sound waves. However, in deep water, the effects of bottom bounce can be more subtle due to the longer water column. This means that the bottom reflection coefficient can have a smaller impact on the propagation of sound waves in deep water.

The bottom reflection coefficient in deep water can be modeled using the same equation as in shallow water:

$$
R = \frac{A_{reflected}}{A_{incident}} = \frac{Z_{water} - Z_{bottom}}{Z_{water} + Z_{bottom}}
$$

However, in deep water, the effects of bottom bounce can be more subtle due to the longer water column. This means that the bottom reflection coefficient can have a smaller impact on the propagation of sound waves in deep water.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles, which are graphical representations of the changes in sound speed with depth in the water column. These profiles can provide valuable insights into the propagation of sound waves in the ocean, including the effects of refraction and bottom bounce.

#### 3.3a Sound Speed Profiles and Layers

Sound speed profiles and layers are crucial tools in acoustical oceanography, providing a detailed understanding of how sound propagates through the water column. These profiles are graphical representations of the changes in sound speed with depth, and they can reveal important information about the properties of the water and the ocean floor.

In the previous sections, we have discussed the effects of refraction and bottom bounce on sound propagation. These effects are often more pronounced in certain layers of the water column, which can be identified in the sound speed profile.

The sound speed profile is typically represented as a function of depth, with the surface of the water at the top and the ocean floor at the bottom. The sound speed is usually plotted in meters per second, and the depth is usually plotted in meters. The resulting curve can provide valuable insights into the properties of the water and the ocean floor.

For example, in a deep water environment, the sound speed profile might show a gradual increase in sound speed with depth, reflecting the increase in pressure and temperature with depth. This increase in sound speed can lead to refraction, which can alter the direction of propagation of sound waves.

In the same deep water environment, the sound speed profile might also show distinct layers, each with its own sound speed. These layers can be caused by changes in the properties of the water, such as temperature, salinity, or density. For example, a layer of warm, less dense water might have a lower sound speed than a layer of cold, denser water.

The sound speed profile can also reveal information about the ocean floor. For example, a layer of sediment or rock with a different sound speed than the water can cause a change in the sound speed profile. This change can be used to identify the depth of the ocean floor and the properties of the ocean floor.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles in more detail, including how they are measured and how they can be used to understand sound propagation in the ocean.

#### 3.3b Sound Speed Profiles and Layers in Shallow Water

The sound speed is usually plotted in meters per second, and the depth is usually plotted in meters.

In shallow water, the sound speed profile can show a more complex pattern than in deep water. This is due to the shorter water column, which allows for more significant changes in sound speed with depth. These changes can be caused by a variety of factors, including temperature, salinity, and the presence of suspended sediments.

For example, in a shallow water environment, the sound speed profile might show a gradual increase in sound speed with depth, reflecting the increase in temperature and salinity with depth. The sound speed profile can also reveal information about the ocean floor. This change can be used to identify the depth of the ocean floor and the properties of the ocean floor.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound speed profiles in more detail, including how they are measured and how they can be used to understand sound propagation in the ocean.

## Chapter 4: Acoustic Tomography

### Introduction

Acoustic tomography, a term coined by physicist Russell V. H. Sutton in 1961, is a powerful tool in the field of acoustical oceanography. It is a non-invasive imaging technique that uses sound waves to create a cross-sectional image of the ocean floor or other submerged structures. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of acoustic tomography, exploring its principles, applications, and the complex mathematical models that underpin it.

The concept of acoustic tomography is based on the reflection and refraction of sound waves. When a sound wave encounters a boundary between two different media, such as water and sediment, part of the wave is reflected back into the water and part is refracted into the sediment. By analyzing the reflected and refracted waves, it is possible to create an image of the sediment layer.

The mathematical models used in acoustic tomography are based on the principles of wave propagation and reflection. These models use variables such as the sound speed in the water and sediment, the thickness of the sediment layer, and the angle of the sound wave to calculate the reflected and refracted waves. These calculations are then used to create an image of the sediment layer.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of acoustic tomography in detail, including the mathematical models used to calculate the reflected and refracted waves. We will also discuss the practical applications of acoustic tomography in oceanography, such as mapping the ocean floor and studying sediment layers. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of acoustic tomography and its role in acoustical oceanography.




#### 3.2b Bent Ray Paths and Sound Bending

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of refraction and how it affects the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. In this section, we will delve deeper into the phenomenon of sound bending, which is a direct result of refraction.

Sound bending, also known as sound deflection, is a phenomenon that occurs when sound waves encounter a boundary between two media with different refractive indices. As we have learned, the refractive index of a medium is a measure of how much it slows down the speed of sound. When a sound wave encounters a boundary, it can change its direction due to the difference in refractive indices between the two media.

The amount of sound bending that occurs depends on the angle of incidence, the refractive indices of the two media, and the wavelength of the sound wave. The mathematical description of sound bending is given by Snell's Law, which we discussed in the previous section.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, sound bending can have significant implications. For instance, it can cause sound waves to deviate from their original path, leading to changes in the direction of propagation. This can be particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple boundaries between different layers of water with varying properties.

Sound bending can also affect the intensity of sound waves. As sound waves bend, they can spread out or concentrate, leading to changes in the intensity of the sound. This can have implications for the detection and localization of sound sources in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound bending in more detail, including its effects on the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will also discuss some of the practical applications of sound bending in acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.2c Refraction and Sound Propagation

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concepts of refraction and sound bending. Now, we will explore how these phenomena affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

As we have learned, sound waves travel through the ocean at a speed determined by the properties of the water. These properties include temperature, salinity, and pressure. When a sound wave encounters a boundary between two layers of water with different properties, it can change its direction due to refraction.

The amount of refraction that occurs depends on the difference in refractive indices between the two layers. The refractive index of a medium is a measure of how much it slows down the speed of sound. In the ocean, the refractive index can vary significantly due to changes in temperature, salinity, and pressure.

The mathematical description of refraction is given by Snell's Law, which we discussed in the previous section. This law describes how the angle of incidence of a sound wave changes when it encounters a boundary between two media.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, refraction can have significant implications. For instance, it can cause sound waves to deviate from their original path, leading to changes in the direction of propagation. This can be particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple boundaries between different layers of water with varying properties.

Refraction can also affect the intensity of sound waves. As sound waves bend, they can spread out or concentrate, leading to changes in the intensity of the sound. This can have implications for the detection and localization of sound sources in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound bending in more detail, including its effects on the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will also discuss some of the practical applications of sound bending in acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.3a Sound Propagation in Shallow Water

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concepts of refraction and sound bending, and how these phenomena affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. Now, we will focus on the propagation of sound waves in shallow water.

Shallow water refers to the upper layer of the ocean, typically up to a depth of 200 meters. In this layer, the water is generally warmer and less dense than the deeper layers. The properties of the water in this layer can vary significantly, leading to complex sound propagation characteristics.

The speed of sound in shallow water is primarily determined by the temperature and salinity of the water. As we have learned, temperature and salinity can affect the density of the water, which in turn affects the speed of sound. The speed of sound in shallow water can vary significantly due to these variations in temperature and salinity.

The propagation of sound waves in shallow water is also affected by the bottom of the ocean. The bottom can be hard or soft, smooth or rough, and can have a significant impact on the propagation of sound waves. For instance, a hard, smooth bottom can reflect sound waves back into the water column, leading to multiple reflections and refractions.

The mathematical description of sound propagation in shallow water is complex and involves several equations. These equations take into account the effects of temperature, salinity, and the bottom of the ocean on the propagation of sound waves.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound propagation in deep water, where the water is generally colder and denser than in shallow water. We will also discuss the implications of these differences for acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.3b Sound Propagation in Deep Water

In the previous section, we discussed the propagation of sound waves in shallow water. Now, we will shift our focus to deep water, where the water is generally colder and denser than in shallow water.

Deep water refers to the lower layers of the ocean, typically beyond a depth of 200 meters. In this layer, the water is generally colder and denser than the shallow water layer, leading to different sound propagation characteristics.

The speed of sound in deep water is primarily determined by the temperature and salinity of the water, similar to shallow water. However, the effects of temperature and salinity on the speed of sound are more pronounced in deep water due to the higher density of the water. This can lead to significant variations in the speed of sound with depth.

The propagation of sound waves in deep water is also affected by the bottom of the ocean. However, the impact of the bottom on sound propagation is less significant in deep water due to the greater depth. This is because the bottom is typically further away from the sound source, leading to weaker reflections and refractions.

The mathematical description of sound propagation in deep water is also complex and involves several equations. These equations take into account the effects of temperature, salinity, and the bottom of the ocean on the propagation of sound waves. However, the equations are more complex in deep water due to the greater variations in the properties of the water.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound propagation in the ocean as a whole, taking into account both shallow and deep water. We will also discuss the implications of these differences for acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.3c Sound Propagation in Complex Ocean Environments

In the previous sections, we have discussed the propagation of sound waves in shallow and deep water. Now, we will delve into the complexities of sound propagation in various ocean environments.

The ocean is a vast and dynamic environment, with a wide range of physical characteristics that can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves. These characteristics include variations in temperature, salinity, and density, as well as the presence of various types of bottoms and interfaces between different layers of water.

One of the most complex ocean environments is the Arctic Ocean. The Arctic Ocean is characterized by a high degree of variability in its physical properties, due to the influence of sea ice and the presence of deep basins. The sound speed in the Arctic Ocean can vary significantly with depth, with a mean sound speed of 1480 m/s in the upper 100 m and a mean sound speed of 1530 m/s in the deep basins.

The propagation of sound waves in the Arctic Ocean is also affected by the presence of sea ice. Sea ice can cause significant scattering of sound waves, leading to a reduction in the range of sound propagation. However, the presence of sea ice can also lead to the formation of sound channels, which can enhance the propagation of sound waves.

The mathematical description of sound propagation in complex ocean environments is a challenging task. It requires the use of advanced equations that take into account the effects of various physical properties of the ocean on the propagation of sound waves. These equations are often based on the principles of acoustics and fluid dynamics, and they can be quite complex due to the non-linear nature of the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound propagation in the ocean as a whole, taking into account the complexities of various ocean environments. We will also discuss the implications of these complexities for acoustical oceanography.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of underwater sound propagation, a critical aspect of acoustical oceanography. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, including the effects of temperature, salinity, and pressure. We have also examined the various factors that can influence sound propagation, such as the presence of marine life and underwater structures.

We have learned that sound waves travel faster in warmer water and slower in colder water, and that salinity can also affect the speed of sound. We have also discovered that pressure can cause significant changes in the wavelength and frequency of sound waves, leading to phenomena such as Doppler shifting and bending of sound waves.

Furthermore, we have discussed the importance of understanding sound propagation in the context of ocean exploration and research. By understanding how sound waves behave in the ocean, we can better interpret the data collected by acoustic instruments and improve our understanding of the ocean's structure and dynamics.

In conclusion, the study of underwater sound propagation is a complex and fascinating field that plays a crucial role in oceanography. It is a field that is constantly evolving, with new discoveries and advancements being made on a regular basis. As we continue to explore and study the ocean, our understanding of underwater sound propagation will only continue to grow and evolve.

## Chapter 4: Sound Scattering and Absorption

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex environment, teeming with life and filled with a myriad of sounds. From the gentle lapping of waves on the shore to the deep, resonant calls of whales, the ocean is a symphony of sound. However, understanding how these sounds are created, propagated, and absorbed is a complex task. In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of sound scattering and absorption in the ocean.

Sound scattering and absorption are fundamental processes that govern the behavior of sound waves in the ocean. Sound scattering refers to the process by which sound waves are deflected or redirected as they encounter different mediums, such as water, air, or marine life. This process is crucial in determining the direction and intensity of sound waves in the ocean.

On the other hand, sound absorption is the process by which sound waves are absorbed by the medium they are traveling through. In the ocean, this can occur due to the presence of marine life, such as fish and whales, as well as due to the properties of the water itself. Understanding sound absorption is key to predicting how sound waves will behave in the ocean.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles behind sound scattering and absorption, and how these processes interact with the unique properties of the ocean. We will also discuss the implications of these processes for various applications, such as underwater communication and navigation.

As we journey through this chapter, we will also touch upon the mathematical models that describe sound scattering and absorption. These models, often expressed in terms of equations, provide a quantitative framework for understanding and predicting these processes. For instance, the scattering cross-section, often denoted as `$\sigma(\theta, \phi)$`, is a key parameter in describing how sound waves are scattered.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of the complex processes of sound scattering and absorption in the ocean, and their importance in the field of acoustical oceanography. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the fascinating world of underwater sound.




#### 3.2c Refraction and Sound Propagation

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of refraction and sound bending. In this section, we will explore the relationship between refraction and sound propagation in more detail.

Refraction is a fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography. It is the phenomenon by which sound waves change their direction when passing from one medium to another. This change in direction is due to the difference in the refractive indices of the two media. The refractive index of a medium is a measure of how much it slows down the speed of sound.

The relationship between refraction and sound propagation can be described mathematically using Snell's Law. This law states that the ratio of the sine of the angle of incidence to the sine of the angle of refraction is equal to the inverse ratio of the refractive indices of the two media. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
n_1 \sin(\theta_1) = n_2 \sin(\theta_2)
$$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the refractive indices of the two media, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, refraction can have significant implications. For instance, it can cause sound waves to deviate from their original path, leading to changes in the direction of propagation. This can be particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple boundaries between different layers of water with varying properties.

Refraction can also affect the intensity of sound waves. As sound waves bend, they can spread out or concentrate, leading to changes in the intensity of the sound. This can have implications for the detection and localization of sound sources in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound bending in more detail, including its effects on the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will also discuss some of the practical applications of sound bending in acoustical oceanography.

n_1 \sin(\theta_1) = n_2 \sin(\theta_2)
$$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the refractive indices of the two media, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, refraction can have significant implications. For instance, it can cause sound waves to deviate from their original path, leading to changes in the direction of propagation. This can be particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple boundaries between different layers of water with varying properties.

Refraction can also affect the intensity of sound waves. As sound waves bend, they can spread out or concentrate, leading to changes in the intensity of the sound. This can have implications for the detection and localization of sound sources in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound bending in more detail, including its effects on the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will also discuss some of the practical applications of sound bending in acoustical oceanography.

#### 3.2q Refraction and Sound Propagation

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of refraction and sound bending. In this section, we will explore the relationship between refraction and sound propagation in more detail.

Refraction is a fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography. It is the phenomenon by which sound waves change their direction when passing from one medium to another. This change in direction is due to the difference in the refractive indices of the two media. The refractive index of a medium is a measure of how much it slows down the speed of sound.

The relationship between refraction and sound propagation can be described mathematically using Snell's Law. This law states that the ratio of the sine of the angle of incidence to the sine of the angle of refraction is equal to the inverse ratio of the refractive indices of the two media. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
n_1 \sin(\theta_1) = n_2 \sin(\theta_2)
$$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the refractive indices of the two media, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, refraction can have significant implications. For instance, it can cause sound waves to deviate from their original path, leading to changes in the direction of propagation. This can be particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple boundaries between different layers of water with varying properties.

Refraction can also affect the intensity of sound waves. As sound waves bend, they can spread out or concentrate, leading to changes in the intensity of the sound. This can have implications for the detection and localization of sound sources in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sound bending in more detail, including its effects on the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will also discuss some of the practical applications of sound bending in acoustical oceanography.

Refraction is a fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography. It is the phenomenon by which sound waves change their direction when passing from one medium to another. $$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the refractive indices of the two media, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively.

#### 3.3a Rayleigh Scattering

Rayleigh scattering is a fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with small particles in the water, causing the waves to deviate from their original path. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Rayleigh scattering is given by the Rayleigh scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Rayleigh scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Rayleigh scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Rayleigh scattering plays a crucial role in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter many small particles, causing significant scattering. Understanding Rayleigh scattering is therefore essential for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean and for designing effective acoustic communication systems.

#### 3.3b Mie Scattering

Mie scattering is another fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with particles that are larger than the wavelength of the sound. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Mie scattering is given by the Mie scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Mie scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Mie scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Mie scattering plays a crucial role in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is particularly important in the deep ocean, where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter many particles larger than the wavelength of the sound. Understanding Mie scattering is therefore essential for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean and for designing effective acoustic communication systems.

#### 3.3c Scattering in the Ocean

Scattering in the ocean is a complex phenomenon that involves the interaction of sound waves with various particles and structures in the water. This interaction can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves, particularly in the deep ocean where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter a variety of particles and structures.

The scattering of sound waves in the ocean can be broadly categorized into two types: Rayleigh scattering and Mie scattering. As we have seen in the previous sections, Rayleigh scattering occurs when sound waves interact with particles that are much smaller than the wavelength of the sound, while Mie scattering occurs when sound waves interact with particles that are larger than the wavelength of the sound.

The scattering of sound waves in the ocean is also influenced by the properties of the water, such as its salinity, temperature, and density. These properties can affect the refractive index of the water, which in turn affects the scattering of sound waves. For example, changes in salinity can cause changes in the refractive index of the water, which can affect the scattering of sound waves.

In addition to the scattering of sound waves, the ocean also exhibits a phenomenon known as sound bending. This is due to the fact that sound waves travel at different speeds in different layers of the ocean, leading to changes in the direction of the sound waves. Sound bending can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean, particularly in the deep ocean where sound waves can travel long distances and encounter multiple layers of water with different properties.

The study of scattering and sound bending in the ocean is crucial for understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean. It is also essential for designing effective acoustic communication systems, as it can help predict the propagation of sound waves in the ocean and identify potential sources of interference.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the topic of sound bending and explore its implications for acoustical oceanography.




#### 3.3b Mie Scattering

Mie scattering is another fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with particles of comparable size to the wavelength of the sound, such as plankton, bubbles, and other marine organisms. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Mie scattering is given by the Mie scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Mie scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Mie scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Mie scattering is particularly important due to its role in the scattering of sound waves. Sound waves in the ocean interact with a variety of particles, including plankton, bubbles, and other marine organisms. These particles, due to their size and composition, can scatter sound waves in a manner that is described by the Mie scattering formula. Understanding Mie scattering is therefore crucial for understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.3c Scattering in the Ocean

Scattering in the ocean is a complex phenomenon that involves the interaction of sound waves with various particles and structures in the water. This interaction can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves, particularly in the deep ocean where the water column is dense and filled with a variety of particles.

One of the key factors that influence scattering in the ocean is the size of the particles. As we have seen in the previous sections, both Rayleigh and Mie scattering are dependent on the size of the particles. In the ocean, particles can range in size from microscopic plankton to large marine organisms. The size of these particles can significantly affect the amount and direction of scattering.

Another important factor is the composition of the particles. The refractive index of a particle, which is a measure of how much it bends light or sound waves, is dependent on its composition. Different particles, such as plankton, bubbles, and marine organisms, have different refractive indices. This can affect the amount and direction of scattering.

The depth of the water column also plays a role in scattering. In shallow water, the water column is relatively thin and the particles are closer to the surface. This can lead to more intense scattering, as the particles are closer to the sound source. However, in deep water, the water column is thicker and the particles are further away from the surface. This can lead to less intense scattering, as the particles are further away from the sound source.

The direction of the sound waves also affects scattering. In the ocean, sound waves can travel in any direction, depending on the depth and the presence of structures such as the seafloor or the surface. This can lead to complex scattering patterns, as the sound waves interact with different particles in different directions.

In addition to these factors, the frequency of the sound waves also affects scattering. As we have seen in the previous sections, both Rayleigh and Mie scattering are dependent on the wavelength of the sound waves. This means that different frequencies of sound waves will interact with particles in different ways, leading to different amounts and directions of scattering.

Understanding scattering in the ocean is crucial for understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It can help us predict how sound waves will interact with different particles and structures, and how this will affect the overall propagation of sound in the ocean. This knowledge can be applied in various fields, such as oceanography, marine biology, and acoustics.




#### 3.3c Bragg Scattering

Bragg scattering is another fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with a periodic structure, such as a grating or a layered medium. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Bragg scattering is given by the Bragg scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Bragg scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Bragg scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Bragg scattering can be used to study the properties of the ocean, such as the size and distribution of particles, the refractive index of the water, and the presence of layered structures. By analyzing the scattered sound waves, we can gain valuable insights into the ocean's structure and dynamics.

#### 3.3d Rayleigh Scattering

Rayleigh scattering is another fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with particles that are much smaller than the wavelength of the sound, such as water molecules. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Rayleigh scattering is given by the Rayleigh scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Rayleigh scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Rayleigh scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that smaller particles scatter more strongly than larger particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Rayleigh scattering can be used to study the properties of the ocean, such as the size and distribution of particles, the refractive index of the water, and the presence of layered structures.
