#### 3.3e Raman Scattering

Raman scattering is another fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a type of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with molecules in the water, causing a change in the vibrational energy of the molecules. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of Raman scattering is given by the Raman scattering formula:

$$
\frac{dI}{d\Omega} = \frac{I_0}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave, $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Raman scattering formula shows that the intensity of the scattered wave is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

Raman scattering is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section, which is a measure of the amount of scattering, is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, Raman scattering can be used to study the properties of the ocean, such as the size and distribution of particles, the refractive index of the water, and the presence of layered structures. By analyzing the scattered sound waves, we can gain valuable insights into the ocean's structure and dynamics.

#### 3.3f Scattering Cross-Section

The scattering cross-section is a fundamental concept in acoustical oceanography, particularly in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It is a measure of the amount of scattering that occurs when sound waves interact with particles in the water. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it can significantly affect the propagation of sound over long distances.

The mathematical description of the scattering cross-section is given by the formula:

$$
\sigma = \frac{dI}{I_0}
$$

where $\sigma$ is the scattering cross-section, $dI$ is the change in intensity of the scattered wave, and $I_0$ is the intensity of the incident wave. This formula shows that the scattering cross-section is a dimensionless quantity, and it represents the fraction of the incident wave that is scattered.

The scattering cross-section is dependent on the size of the scattering particles, the wavelength of the incident wave, and the refractive index of the particles. The formula for the scattering cross-section is given by the Rayleigh scattering formula:

$$
\sigma = \frac{1}{k^4 r^2} \left(\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}\right)^4 \left|\frac{m^2 - 1}{m^2 + 2}\right|^2 \frac{P_l(m)}{l(l+1)} \left(\frac{kr}{\sin\theta}\right)^{2l+3} \cos^{2l+3}(\theta/2)
$$

where $k$ is the wave number, $r$ is the distance from the scattering particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave, $m$ is the complex refractive index of the particle, $P_l(m)$ is the Legendre polynomial of degree $l$, and $l$ is the angular momentum quantum number.

The Rayleigh scattering formula shows that the scattering cross-section is inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. This means that shorter wavelengths, such as high-frequency sound waves, are scattered more strongly than longer wavelengths. This is why high-frequency sound waves are more likely to be scattered out of the water column, reducing their range and effectiveness in long-distance communication.

The scattering cross-section is also dependent on the size of the scattering particles. The formula shows that the scattering cross-section is proportional to the sixth power of the particle size. This means that larger particles scatter more strongly than smaller particles, which can have significant implications for the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

In the context of acoustical oceanography, the scattering cross-section can be used to study the properties of the ocean, such as the size and distribution of particles, the refractive index of the water, and the presence of layered structures. By analyzing the scattered sound waves, we can gain valuable insights into the ocean's structure and dynamics.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of underwater sound propagation, a critical aspect of acoustical oceanography. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, including the effects of temperature, salinity, and pressure on sound propagation. We have also examined the various factors that can influence the transmission of sound, such as the presence of bubbles, sediment, and marine life.

We have learned that sound propagation in the ocean is a highly dynamic and interactive process, influenced by a multitude of factors. The understanding of these factors is crucial for accurate prediction of sound propagation, which is essential for a wide range of applications, from underwater communication to marine navigation and exploration.

The study of underwater sound propagation is a vast and complex field, and this chapter has only scratched the surface. However, it has provided a solid foundation for further exploration into this fascinating area of acoustical oceanography.

## Chapter 4: Sound Propagation in Shallow Water

### Introduction

The study of sound propagation in shallow water is a critical aspect of acoustical oceanography. This chapter will delve into the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of sound waves in shallow water bodies, and how these principles are applied in the field of acoustical oceanography.

Shallow water, defined as water bodies with a depth of less than 20 meters, presents unique challenges and opportunities for sound propagation. The interaction of sound waves with the water surface, the seafloor, and the atmospheric boundary layer can significantly alter the characteristics of the sound signal. Understanding these interactions is crucial for predicting the behavior of sound waves in shallow water, and for designing effective acoustical systems for oceanographic research and applications.

We will explore the mathematical models that describe sound propagation in shallow water, including the effects of surface and bottom reflections, and the influence of the atmospheric boundary layer. These models, expressed in terms of the wavelength of the sound, the depth of the water body, and the properties of the water and the atmosphere, provide a quantitative framework for understanding and predicting sound propagation in shallow water.

We will also discuss the practical implications of sound propagation in shallow water, including the design of acoustical instruments for oceanographic research, and the interpretation of acoustical data collected in shallow water environments.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to the topic of sound propagation in shallow water, suitable for both students and professionals in the field of acoustical oceanography. It is our hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for those seeking to understand and apply the principles of sound propagation in shallow water.




#### 3.4a Absorption Coefficients

Absorption is a critical aspect of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the process by which sound waves lose energy as they travel through the water. This loss of energy is due to the interaction of the sound waves with the water molecules, which causes the molecules to vibrate and dissipate the energy. The amount of absorption that occurs depends on the frequency of the sound wave and the properties of the water.

The absorption coefficient, denoted by $\alpha$, is a measure of the amount of absorption that occurs per unit length of the water column. It is defined as the ratio of the absorbed power to the incident power, and is given by the equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{P_{abs}}{P_{inc}}
$$

where $P_{abs}$ is the absorbed power and $P_{inc}$ is the incident power. The absorption coefficient is a dimensionless quantity, and it can range from 0 (no absorption) to 1 (complete absorption).

The absorption coefficient is dependent on the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. Generally, higher frequencies and higher temperatures result in higher absorption coefficients. This is because higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, which means they interact more strongly with the water molecules. Similarly, higher temperatures increase the kinetic energy of the water molecules, which increases the amount of energy that can be absorbed.

The absorption coefficient also increases with depth in the water column. This is because the pressure increases with depth, which increases the density of the water and the interaction between the sound waves and the water molecules. This is why deep-sea sound propagation is more difficult than surface sound propagation.

The absorption coefficient is a crucial parameter in acoustical oceanography, as it determines the range and effectiveness of sound waves in the ocean. It is used in the design of sonar systems, and in the study of ocean acoustics. Understanding the factors that influence the absorption coefficient is essential for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of sound attenuation, which is closely related to absorption.

#### 3.4b Absorption in Water

The absorption of sound waves in water is a complex process that involves the interaction of the sound waves with the water molecules. This interaction is influenced by several factors, including the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column.

The absorption of sound waves in water can be described by the equation:

$$
\alpha = \alpha_0 \left(\frac{f}{f_0}\right)^{\gamma}
$$

where $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, $\alpha_0$ is the absorption coefficient at a reference frequency $f_0$, $f$ is the frequency of the sound wave, and $\gamma$ is a constant that depends on the properties of the water. This equation shows that the absorption coefficient increases with frequency, which is why higher frequencies are absorbed more strongly than lower frequencies.

The absorption coefficient also increases with temperature and salinity. This is because higher temperatures and higher salinities increase the kinetic energy of the water molecules, which increases the amount of energy that can be absorbed. The absorption coefficient also increases with depth in the water column, due to the increase in pressure and density with depth.

The absorption of sound waves in water is a crucial aspect of acoustical oceanography. It determines the range and effectiveness of sound waves in the ocean, and it is a key factor in the design of sonar systems. Understanding the factors that influence the absorption of sound waves in water is essential for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of sound attenuation, which is closely related to absorption.

#### 3.4c Absorption in Air

The absorption of sound waves in air is another important aspect of acoustical oceanography. Unlike water, air is a non-homogeneous medium, and the absorption of sound waves in air is influenced by a variety of factors, including the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and pressure of the air, and the presence of particles and gases in the air.

The absorption of sound waves in air can be described by the equation:

$$
\alpha = \alpha_0 \left(\frac{f}{f_0}\right)^{\gamma}
$$

where $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, $\alpha_0$ is the absorption coefficient at a reference frequency $f_0$, $f$ is the frequency of the sound wave, and $\gamma$ is a constant that depends on the properties of the air. This equation shows that the absorption coefficient increases with frequency, similar to the case of water.

The absorption coefficient also increases with temperature and pressure. This is because higher temperatures and higher pressures increase the kinetic energy of the air molecules, which increases the amount of energy that can be absorbed. The absorption coefficient also increases with the presence of particles and gases in the air, due to the increased scattering and absorption of sound waves by these particles and gases.

The absorption of sound waves in air is a crucial aspect of acoustical oceanography. It determines the range and effectiveness of sound waves in the ocean, and it is a key factor in the design of sonar systems. Understanding the factors that influence the absorption of sound waves in air is essential for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of sound attenuation, which is closely related to absorption.




#### 3.4b Attenuation Mechanisms

Attenuation is another important aspect of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the process by which the intensity of sound waves decreases as they travel through the water. This loss of intensity is due to the absorption of sound energy by the water, as well as other mechanisms such as scattering and reflection.

The attenuation coefficient, denoted by $\alpha$, is a measure of the amount of attenuation that occurs per unit length of the water column. It is defined as the ratio of the attenuated intensity to the incident intensity, and is given by the equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{I_{att}}{I_{inc}}
$$

where $I_{att}$ is the attenuated intensity and $I_{inc}$ is the incident intensity. The attenuation coefficient is a dimensionless quantity, and it can range from 0 (no attenuation) to 1 (complete attenuation).

The attenuation coefficient is dependent on the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. Generally, higher frequencies and higher temperatures result in higher attenuation coefficients. This is because higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, which means they interact more strongly with the water molecules and are more likely to be absorbed or scattered. Similarly, higher temperatures increase the kinetic energy of the water molecules, which increases the amount of energy that can be absorbed or scattered.

The attenuation coefficient also increases with depth in the water column. This is because the pressure increases with depth, which increases the density of the water and the interaction between the sound waves and the water molecules. This is why deep-sea sound propagation is more difficult than surface sound propagation.

There are several mechanisms that contribute to attenuation in the ocean. These include absorption, scattering, and reflection. Absorption, as discussed earlier, is the process by which sound energy is absorbed by the water molecules. Scattering occurs when sound waves are deflected from their original path due to interactions with particles or boundaries in the water. Reflection occurs when sound waves are bounced back from the surface or the seafloor.

The total attenuation coefficient, $\alpha_{total}$, is the sum of the attenuation coefficients due to each of these mechanisms. It can be expressed as:

$$
\alpha_{total} = \alpha_{abs} + \alpha_{scat} + \alpha_{refl}
$$

where $\alpha_{abs}$ is the attenuation coefficient due to absorption, $\alpha_{scat}$ is the attenuation coefficient due to scattering, and $\alpha_{refl}$ is the attenuation coefficient due to reflection.

Understanding these attenuation mechanisms is crucial for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, and for designing effective sonar systems.

#### 3.4c Absorption and Attenuation Models

To better understand the mechanisms of absorption and attenuation in the ocean, we can use mathematical models. These models can help us predict the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, and can be used to design effective sonar systems.

One such model is the Kolsky model, which is used to calculate the attenuation and dispersion of sound waves in the ocean. The Kolsky model is based on the assumption that the ocean can be modeled as a non-radiative dielectric waveguide. This model is particularly useful for frequencies below 100 Hz, where the effects of absorption and attenuation are more pronounced.

The Kolsky model can be modified to account for the effects of absorption and attenuation. For example, the choice of the lowest frequency in the frequency band, $w_{r}$, can introduce phase errors when the model is used as an inverse Q filter. To correct for this, the phase velocity formula in the basic Kolsky model can be modified by using the highest frequency $w_{h}$ as a reference.

Another model that can be used to calculate absorption and attenuation is the Bjørn Ursin and Tommy Toverud model. This model is based on the assumption that the ocean can be modeled as a non-radiative dielectric waveguide, and it is particularly useful for frequencies above 100 Hz.

The Bjørn Ursin and Tommy Toverud model can be modified to account for the effects of absorption and attenuation. For example, the model can be modified to account for the effects of absorption and attenuation on the phase velocity of the sound waves. This can be done by modifying the phase velocity formula in the basic model, as was done in the Kolsky model.

In addition to these models, there are also several numerical schemes available for calculating the attenuation of sound waves in the ocean. These schemes are based on the eikonal equation, and they can be used to calculate the attenuation of sound waves for a wide range of frequencies and conditions.

For example, a simple second order scheme can be used to calculate the attenuation of sound waves in the ocean. This scheme is particularly useful for frequencies below 100 Hz, where the effects of absorption and attenuation are more pronounced.

In conclusion, understanding the mechanisms of absorption and attenuation in the ocean is crucial for predicting the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, and for designing effective sonar systems. Mathematical models and numerical schemes can be used to calculate the attenuation and dispersion of sound waves in the ocean, and these models can be modified to account for the effects of absorption and attenuation.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of underwater sound propagation. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, and how these principles can be applied to understand and predict the behavior of sound waves in the ocean. We have also examined the various factors that can affect sound propagation, such as temperature, salinity, and the presence of marine life.

We have learned that sound waves travel faster in warmer water than in colder water, and that the speed of sound is affected by the salinity of the water. We have also learned that the presence of marine life can affect the propagation of sound waves, particularly in the case of large marine mammals.

We have also discussed the importance of understanding underwater sound propagation in various fields, such as oceanography, marine biology, and naval warfare. By understanding how sound waves behave in the ocean, we can better understand the ocean itself, and the life that inhabits it.

In conclusion, the study of underwater sound propagation is a complex and fascinating field that has many practical applications. By understanding the principles that govern the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, we can gain a deeper understanding of the ocean itself, and the life that inhabits it.

## Chapter 4: Reverberation

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex acoustic environment, filled with a myriad of sounds that echo and reverberate across its vast expanse. This chapter, "Reverberation," delves into the fascinating world of underwater sound and its unique characteristics. 

Reverberation, in the context of acoustics, refers to the reflection and repetition of sound waves. In the ocean, this phenomenon is particularly intriguing due to the unique properties of water and the marine environment. The speed of sound in water is much faster than in air, and the density of water allows for sound waves to travel much greater distances. This results in a complex interplay of sound waves, where a single sound can be reflected, refracted, and reverberated multiple times before it dissipates.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of reverberation in the ocean, including the factors that influence it such as water depth, temperature, and salinity. We will also delve into the mathematical models that describe reverberation, such as the impulse response function and the reverberation time. 

We will also discuss the practical applications of reverberation in acoustical oceanography, such as in sonar technology and underwater acoustics. Understanding reverberation is crucial in these fields, as it can help us to better interpret the sounds we hear in the ocean and to predict how they will behave.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of reverberation in the ocean, from its fundamental principles to its practical applications. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of acoustical oceanography, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of this fascinating aspect of underwater sound.




#### 3.4c Frequency and Depth Dependence

The frequency and depth dependence of underwater sound propagation is a crucial aspect of acoustical oceanography. As we have seen in the previous section, the attenuation coefficient, denoted by $\alpha$, is dependent on the frequency of the sound wave. This frequency dependence is also reflected in the absorption coefficient, denoted by $\beta$.

The absorption coefficient, $\beta$, is defined as the ratio of the absorbed intensity to the incident intensity, and is given by the equation:

$$
\beta = \frac{I_{abs}}{I_{inc}}
$$

where $I_{abs}$ is the absorbed intensity and $I_{inc}$ is the incident intensity. The absorption coefficient is also a dimensionless quantity, and it can range from 0 (no absorption) to 1 (complete absorption).

The absorption coefficient is dependent on the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. Generally, higher frequencies and higher temperatures result in higher absorption coefficients. This is because higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, which means they interact more strongly with the water molecules and are more likely to be absorbed. Similarly, higher temperatures increase the kinetic energy of the water molecules, which increases the amount of energy that can be absorbed.

The absorption coefficient also increases with depth in the water column. This is because the pressure increases with depth, which increases the density of the water and the interaction between the sound waves and the water molecules. This is why deep-sea sound propagation is more difficult than surface sound propagation.

The frequency and depth dependence of the absorption coefficient can be described by the equation:

$$
\beta = \beta_0 + \beta_1 f + \beta_2 d
$$

where $\beta_0$ is the absorption coefficient at a reference frequency and depth, $\beta_1$ is the frequency coefficient, and $\beta_2$ is the depth coefficient. The frequency coefficient, $\beta_1$, and the depth coefficient, $\beta_2$, can be determined experimentally.

In the next section, we will explore the effects of these frequency and depth dependencies on underwater sound propagation, and how they can be used to study the ocean.

## Chapter 4: Acoustic Tomography

### Introduction

Acoustic tomography, a powerful tool in the field of acoustical oceanography, is the focus of this chapter. This technique, which uses sound waves to create images of the ocean, has revolutionized our understanding of the underwater world. It has allowed us to peer beneath the surface of the ocean, revealing the complex and dynamic nature of the ocean floor, the water column, and the marine life that inhabits it.

The term "tomography" is derived from the Greek words "tomos" meaning "slice" and "graphein" meaning "to write". In the context of acoustics, tomography refers to the process of creating a two-dimensional image of a three-dimensional object. In the case of acoustic tomography, the object of interest is the ocean, and the image is created using sound waves.

The basic principle of acoustic tomography is simple: sound waves are emitted into the water, and the time it takes for these waves to return is measured. By analyzing the time delay and the changes in the sound waves' frequency, a picture of the ocean can be constructed. This picture, or image, is a representation of the ocean's properties at different depths.

In this chapter, we will delve into the intricacies of acoustic tomography, exploring its principles, techniques, and applications. We will discuss how sound waves interact with the ocean, and how these interactions can be used to create images of the ocean. We will also examine the challenges and limitations of acoustic tomography, and how these can be overcome.

Acoustic tomography is a powerful tool in the hands of oceanographers, providing a non-invasive means of studying the ocean. It has been used to map the ocean floor, to study marine life, and to monitor changes in the ocean over time. As we continue to explore and understand the ocean, acoustic tomography will undoubtedly play a crucial role.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of underwater sound propagation. We have learned about the unique properties of sound in water, including its speed, wavelength, and attenuation. We have also delved into the various factors that can affect sound propagation, such as temperature, salinity, and pressure. By understanding these concepts, we can better appreciate the complex dynamics of sound in the ocean and its role in various oceanographic processes.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the medium in which sound travels. Water is a denser and more viscous medium than air, which leads to significant differences in sound propagation. For example, the speed of sound in water is approximately four times faster than in air, and its wavelength is correspondingly shorter. This means that sound waves can travel much greater distances in the ocean, making it a powerful tool for long-range communication and navigation.

Another important concept we have explored is the attenuation of sound in water. Attenuation refers to the loss of sound energy as it travels through a medium. In water, sound can be attenuated by absorption, scattering, and reflection. These processes can significantly impact the range and clarity of sound, making it a challenge to accurately predict sound propagation in the ocean. However, by understanding these processes and their underlying mechanisms, we can develop more accurate models and techniques for predicting sound propagation.

In conclusion, underwater sound propagation is a complex and fascinating field that plays a crucial role in oceanography. By understanding the unique properties of sound in water and the factors that affect its propagation, we can gain a deeper appreciation for the role of sound in the ocean and its impact on various oceanographic processes.

## Chapter: Acoustical Oceanography: Exploring the Depths of Sound in the Ocean

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of sound and its properties in the ocean. We learned about the different types of sound sources and how they interact with the water column. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the topic of sound propagation in the ocean. Specifically, we will focus on the effects of temperature and salinity on sound transmission.

Temperature and salinity are two important physical properties of seawater that can greatly impact the behavior of sound waves. Temperature refers to the average kinetic energy of the water molecules, while salinity refers to the concentration of dissolved salts in the water. These properties can vary greatly in different regions of the ocean, and understanding their effects on sound propagation is crucial for accurately predicting and interpreting acoustic signals.

In this chapter, we will explore the relationship between temperature and salinity and how they affect the speed of sound in the ocean. We will also discuss the concept of sound absorption and how it is influenced by these properties. Additionally, we will examine the effects of temperature and salinity on the attenuation of sound waves, which is the loss of sound energy as it travels through the water.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the complex interactions between sound, temperature, and salinity in the ocean. This knowledge will be essential for anyone working in the field of acoustical oceanography, as well as anyone interested in learning more about the fascinating world of underwater sound. So let us dive in and explore the depths of sound in the ocean.

Design an experiment to measure the attenuation of sound in a water tank. What factors would you need to consider and how would you account for them?

These properties can vary greatly in different regions of the ocean, and understanding their effects on sound propagation is crucial for accurately predicting and interpreting acoustic signals.

In this chapter, we will explore the relationship between temperature and salinity and how they affect the speed of sound in the ocean. Additionally, we will examine the effects of temperature and salinity on the attenuation of sound waves, which is the loss of sound energy as it travels through the water.

### Section: 4.1 Time-Frequency Analysis

Time-frequency analysis is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography that allows us to study the temporal and spectral characteristics of acoustic signals. It is particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time. In this section, we will explore the concept of time-frequency analysis and its applications in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1a Fourier Transform and Spectrogram

The Fourier Transform is a mathematical tool that decomposes a signal into its constituent frequencies. It is a fundamental concept in signal processing and is widely used in acoustical oceanography. The Fourier Transform of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
X(f) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)e^{-j2\pi ft} dt
$$

where $X(f)$ is the Fourier Transform of $x(t)$, $f$ is the frequency, and $j$ is the imaginary unit. The Fourier Transform provides a frequency domain representation of the signal, where each frequency component is represented by a complex number.

The Spectrogram is a time-frequency representation of a signal. It is obtained by computing the Fourier Transform of the signal at different time intervals. The resulting spectrum is then plotted as a function of time and frequency, providing a detailed view of the signal's frequency content over time. The Spectrogram is particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

The Spectrogram can be computed using the following MATLAB code:

```
% Compute the Fourier Transform of the signal at different time intervals
X = fft(x, N); % N is the number of samples in the signal
T = 1/fs; % fs is the sampling rate
t = (0:N-1)*T; % time vector

% Plot the Spectrogram
figure;
plot(t, abs(X).^2); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Time (t)');
ylabel('Frequency (f)');
title('Spectrogram');
```

The Spectrogram provides a detailed view of the signal's frequency content over time, allowing us to study the temporal and spectral characteristics of the signal. It is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, particularly in the analysis of non-stationary signals.

In the next section, we will explore another important time-frequency analysis technique: the Wavelet Transform.

#### 4.1b Wavelet Transform and Wavelet Spectrum

The Wavelet Transform is another powerful tool in time-frequency analysis. Unlike the Fourier Transform, which provides a frequency domain representation of the signal, the Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation. This makes it particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

The Wavelet Transform of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
X(a, b) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)\psi^*_{a,b}(t) dt
$$

where $X(a, b)$ is the Wavelet Transform of $x(t)$, $\psi_{a,b}(t)$ is the wavelet function, $a$ is the scale parameter, and $b$ is the translation parameter. The wavelet function $\psi_{a,b}(t)$ is a function that varies in time and frequency, allowing us to analyze the signal at different scales and frequencies.

The Wavelet Spectrum is a time-scale representation of the signal. It is obtained by computing the Wavelet Transform of the signal at different scales. The resulting spectrum is then plotted as a function of time and scale, providing a detailed view of the signal's time-scale characteristics. The Wavelet Spectrum is particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

The Wavelet Spectrum can be computed using the following MATLAB code:

```
% Compute the Wavelet Transform of the signal at different scales
X = wavtrans(x, ps, N); % ps is the wavelet function, N is the number of samples in the signal
T = 1/fs; % fs is the sampling rate
t = (0:N-1)*T; % time vector

% Plot the Wavelet Spectrum
figure;
plot(t, abs(X).^2); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Time (t)');
ylabel('Scale (a)');
title('Wavelet Spectrum');
```

The Wavelet Spectrum provides a detailed view of the signal's time-scale characteristics, allowing us to study the temporal and spectral characteristics of the signal. It is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, particularly in the analysis of non-stationary signals.

#### 4.1c Least-Squares Spectral Analysis

The Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA) is a method used to estimate the power spectrum of a signal. It is based on the principle of least squares, which minimizes the sum of the squares of the residuals. The LSSA is particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

The LSSA involves computing the least-squares spectrum, which is the power spectrum of the signal. This is done by computing the least-squares approximation for each frequency in a desired set of frequencies. For each frequency, sine and cosine functions are evaluated at the times corresponding to the data samples, and dot products of the data vector with the sinusoid vectors are taken and appropriately normalized. This process implements a discrete Fourier transform when the data are uniformly spaced in time and the frequencies chosen correspond to integer numbers of cycles over the finite data record.

The LSSA can be implemented in less than a page of MATLAB code. In essence, for each frequency in a desired set of frequencies, we must compute the least-squares spectrum. This involves performing the least-squares approximation for each frequency, taking dot products of the data vector with the sinusoid vectors, and normalizing the results. This process implements a discrete Fourier transform when the data are uniformly spaced in time and the frequencies chosen correspond to integer numbers of cycles over the finite data record.

The LSSA treats each sinusoidal component independently, even though they may not be orthogonal to data points. This is in contrast to the simultaneous or in-context least-squares fit, which can be implemented by solving a matrix equation and partitioning the total data variance between the specified sinusoid frequencies. This method, however, cannot fit more components (sines and cosines) than there are data samples.

The LSSA is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, particularly in the analysis of non-stationary signals. It allows us to estimate the power spectrum of a signal, providing a detailed view of the signal's frequency content over time. This is particularly useful in the study of the depths of sound in the ocean, where the frequency content of the signal can change rapidly over time.

#### 4.1d Applications of Time-Frequency Analysis

Time-frequency analysis has a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. It allows us to study the temporal and spectral characteristics of signals, providing a detailed view of the signal's frequency content over time. This is particularly useful in the analysis of non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

One of the key applications of time-frequency analysis is in the study of ocean acoustics. The ocean is a complex medium, with its acoustic properties changing rapidly over time due to factors such as temperature, salinity, and pressure. Time-frequency analysis allows us to study these changes, providing insights into the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

Another important application of time-frequency analysis is in the study of marine mammals. Marine mammals, such as dolphins and whales, use sound to communicate and navigate. Time-frequency analysis can be used to study the frequency content of these sounds, providing insights into the communication and navigation strategies of these animals.

Time-frequency analysis is also used in the study of ocean currents. The movement of water in the ocean can be studied using acoustic signals, and time-frequency analysis can be used to analyze these signals, providing insights into the dynamics of ocean currents.

In addition, time-frequency analysis is used in the study of ocean noise. The ocean is a noisy environment, with a variety of sources contributing to the overall noise level. Time-frequency analysis can be used to study this noise, providing insights into its sources and characteristics.

Finally, time-frequency analysis is used in the design and testing of acoustic devices. By analyzing the frequency content of signals, we can design devices that operate effectively in the ocean, and test these devices to ensure that they meet performance requirements.

In conclusion, time-frequency analysis is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, with a wide range of applications. It allows us to study the temporal and spectral characteristics of signals, providing insights into the complex dynamics of the ocean.

## Chapter: Acoustic Imaging

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex environment, teeming with life and filled with sounds that tell a myriad of stories. Acoustic imaging is a powerful tool that allows us to explore and understand this underwater world. In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of acoustic imaging, exploring its principles, applications, and the cutting-edge technology that makes it possible.

Acoustic imaging, also known as sonar, is a technique that uses sound waves to create images of the underwater environment. This technology has revolutionized our ability to explore and study the ocean, providing a non-invasive and highly detailed view of the seafloor and the life that inhabits it. From mapping the seafloor to detecting marine life and underwater structures, acoustic imaging has a wide range of applications in oceanography.

In this chapter, we will explore the physics behind acoustic imaging, including how sound waves interact with water and the ocean floor. We will also discuss the various types of acoustic imaging systems, from single-beam to multi-beam systems, and the advantages and disadvantages of each.

We will also delve into the technology behind acoustic imaging, including the use of advanced signal processing techniques and computer algorithms to interpret and analyze the acoustic signals. This includes topics such as beamforming, target detection and classification, and image reconstruction.

Finally, we will discuss the challenges and future directions of acoustic imaging, including the impact of noise and interference, the potential of new technologies, and the role of acoustic imaging in addressing some of the most pressing issues facing the ocean today.

As we journey through this chapter, we will gain a deeper understanding of the power and potential of acoustic imaging, and its crucial role in our exploration and understanding of the ocean. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply someone with a keen interest in the ocean, we hope that this chapter will provide you with a comprehensive and accessible introduction to the fascinating world of acoustic imaging.




#### 4.1b Short-Time Fourier Transform

The Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT) is a variation of the Fourier Transform that allows us to analyze the frequency content of a signal over short time intervals. This is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where signals often exhibit non-stationary behavior.

The STFT of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
Y[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}
$$

where $Y[m,r]$ is the STFT of $x(t)$, $f[n]$ is the signal sample at time $n$, $w[n]$ is the window sample at time $n$, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The STFT provides a time-frequency representation of the signal, where each frequency component is represented by a complex number. This allows us to analyze the frequency content of the signal over short time intervals, providing a more detailed view of the signal's behavior.

The STFT can be computed using the following MATLAB code:

```
% Compute the Short-Time Fourier Transform of the signal
Y = stft(x, fs, L, R); % fs is the sampling rate, L is the separation in time, and R is the number of short-time sections
T = 1/fs; % fs is the sampling rate
t = (0:N-1)*T; % time vector

% Plot the Short-Time Fourier Transform
figure;
plot(t, abs(Y).^2); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Time (t)');
ylabel('Frequency (f)');
title('Short-Time Fourier Transform');
```

The Short-Time Fourier Transform provides a more detailed view of the signal's frequency content over short time intervals.

#### 4.1c Wavelet Transform

The Wavelet Transform is another powerful tool in time-frequency analysis. Unlike the Fourier Transform, which provides a frequency domain representation of a signal, the Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation. This allows us to analyze the time-varying behavior of signals, which is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography.

The Wavelet Transform of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
X(a,b) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)\psi^*_{a,b}(t) dt
$$

where $X(a,b)$ is the Wavelet Transform of $x(t)$, $\psi_{a,b}(t)$ is the wavelet function at time $t$ and scale $a$ and position $b$, and $\psi^*_{a,b}(t)$ is the complex conjugate of the wavelet function.

The Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation of the signal, where each time-scale component is represented by a complex number. This allows us to analyze the time-varying behavior of the signal, providing a more detailed view of the signal's behavior.

The Wavelet Transform can be computed using the following MATLAB code:

```
% Compute the Wavelet Transform of the signal
X = wavetrans(x, fs, a, b); % fs is the sampling rate, a is the scale, and b is the position
T = 1/fs; % fs is the sampling rate
t = (0:N-1)*T; % time vector

% Plot the Wavelet Transform
figure;
plot(t, abs(X).^2); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Time (t)');
ylabel('Scale (a)');
title('Wavelet Transform');
```

The Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation of the signal, allowing us to analyze the time-varying behavior of the signal.

#### 4.1d Spectral Estimation

Spectral Estimation is a technique used to estimate the power spectrum of a signal. It is a crucial tool in acoustical oceanography, as it allows us to estimate the power of different frequencies in a signal. This is particularly useful when dealing with non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

The Power Spectral Density (PSD) of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
P(f) = \lim_{T \to \infty} E\left[ \frac{1}{T} |X(f)|^2 \right]
$$

where $P(f)$ is the Power Spectral Density, $X(f)$ is the Fourier Transform of $x(t)$, and $E[.]$ denotes the expected value.

The PSD provides a frequency domain representation of the signal, where each frequency component is represented by its power. This allows us to estimate the power of different frequencies in the signal, providing a more detailed view of the signal's frequency content.

The PSD can be estimated using the periodogram method, which is given by:

$$
I(f) = \frac{1}{T} |X(f)|^2
$$

where $I(f)$ is the periodogram, $T$ is the time interval, and $X(f)$ is the Fourier Transform of $x(t)$.

The periodogram method provides an unbiased estimate of the PSD, but it is also sensitive to noise. To mitigate this, we can use the Welch method, which is a modified version of the periodogram method. The Welch method divides the signal into non-overlapping segments, and then computes the periodogram for each segment. The resulting periodograms are then averaged to obtain a more robust estimate of the PSD.

The Welch method can be implemented in MATLAB as follows:

```
% Compute the Welch periodogram of the signal
N = length(x); % number of samples
T = 1/fs; % sampling rate
L = 1024; % length of the segments
R = floor(N/L); % number of segments
P = zeros(N/2+1, 1); % preallocate the power spectrum

for r = 1:R
    s = (r-1)*L+1:r*L; % segment indices
    X = fft(x(s)); % compute the Fourier Transform of the segment
    X = X(1:N/2+1); % keep only the positive frequencies
    P(1:N/2+1) = P(1:N/2+1) + (1/T)*(X.*conj(X)); % update the power spectrum
end

P = P/R; % average the power spectrum
P = P(1:N/2+1); % keep only the positive frequencies
P = P(1:N/2); % discard the DC component

% Plot the power spectrum
figure;
plot(fs/2*0:1:fs/2-1, P); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Frequency (Hz)');
ylabel('Power (dB)');
title('Power Spectral Density');
```

The Welch method provides a more robust estimate of the PSD, but it also introduces a loss of resolution due to the segmentation of the signal.

#### 4.1e Time-Frequency Analysis Techniques

Time-Frequency Analysis (TFA) is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, as it allows us to analyze the time-varying frequency content of signals. This is particularly useful when dealing with non-stationary signals, where the frequency content of the signal changes over time.

One of the most commonly used TFA techniques is the Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT). The STFT provides a time-frequency representation of the signal, where each frequency component is represented by its power. This allows us to analyze the frequency content of the signal over short time intervals, providing a more detailed view of the signal's frequency behavior.

The STFT of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
Y[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}
$$

where $Y[m,r]$ is the STFT of $x(t)$, $f[n]$ is the signal sample at time $n$, $w[n]$ is the window sample at time $n$, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The STFT can be implemented in MATLAB as follows:

```
% Compute the Short-Time Fourier Transform of the signal
N = length(x); % number of samples
T = 1/fs; % sampling rate
L = 1024; % length of the segments
R = floor(N/L); % number of segments
Y = zeros(N/2+1, R); % preallocate the STFT

for r = 1:R
    s = (r-1)*L+1:r*L; % segment indices
    X = fft(x(s)); % compute the Fourier Transform of the segment
    X = X(1:N/2+1); % keep only the positive frequencies
    Y(:,r) = Y(:,r) + (1/T)*(X.*conj(X)); % update the STFT
end

Y = Y/R; % average the STFT
Y = Y(1:N/2+1); % keep only the positive frequencies
Y = Y(1:N/2); % discard the DC component

% Plot the Short-Time Fourier Transform
figure;
plot(T*0:T:T*L-T, abs(Y)); % plot the STFT
xlabel('Time (s)');
ylabel('Frequency (Hz)');
title('Short-Time Fourier Transform');
```

Another important TFA technique is the Wavelet Transform. The Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation of the signal, where each time-scale component is represented by its power. This allows us to analyze the time-varying behavior of the signal, providing a more detailed view of the signal's behavior.

The Wavelet Transform of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
X(a,b) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)\psi^*_{a,b}(t) dt
$$

where $X(a,b)$ is the Wavelet Transform of $x(t)$, $\psi_{a,b}(t)$ is the wavelet function at time $t$ and scale $a$ and position $b$, and $\psi^*_{a,b}(t)$ is the complex conjugate of the wavelet function.

The Wavelet Transform can be implemented in MATLAB as follows:

```
% Compute the Wavelet Transform of the signal
N = length(x); % number of samples
T = 1/fs; % sampling rate
a = 2^(-(0:R-1)); % scales
b = (0:N-1)*T; % positions
X = zeros(N/2+1, R); % preallocate the Wavelet Transform

for r = 1:R
    s = (r-1)*L+1:r*L; % segment indices
    X(:,r) = X(:,r) + (1/T)*(x(s).*conj(psi(a,b))); % update the Wavelet Transform
end

X = X/R; % average the Wavelet Transform
X = X(1:N/2+1); % keep only the positive frequencies
X = X(1:N/2); % discard the DC component

% Plot the Wavelet Transform
figure;
plot(T*0:T:T*L-T, abs(X)); % plot the Wavelet Transform
xlabel('Time (s)');
ylabel('Scale (1/s)');
title('Wavelet Transform');
```

In the next section, we will discuss how to use these TFA techniques in the analysis of acoustical oceanography signals.




#### 4.1c Wavelet Transform

The Wavelet Transform is a mathematical tool that allows us to analyze the time-varying behavior of signals. It provides a time-scale representation of a signal, where each scale represents a different time interval. This is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where signals often exhibit non-stationary behavior over different time scales.

The Wavelet Transform of a signal $x(t)$ is given by:

$$
Y[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}
$$

where $Y[m,r]$ is the Wavelet Transform of $x(t)$, $f[n]$ is the signal sample at time $n$, $w[n]$ is the wavelet sample at time $n$, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation of the signal, where each scale is represented by a complex number. This allows us to analyze the time-varying behavior of the signal over different time scales.

The Wavelet Transform can be computed using the following MATLAB code:

```
% Compute the Wavelet Transform of the signal
Y = wavet(x, fs, L, R); % fs is the sampling rate, L is the separation in time, and R is the number of short-time sections
T = 1/fs; % fs is the sampling rate
t = (0:N-1)*T; % time vector

% Plot the Wavelet Transform
figure;
plot(t, abs(Y).^2); % plot the power spectrum
xlabel('Time (t)');
ylabel('Scale (s)');
title('Wavelet Transform');
```

The Wavelet Transform provides a more detailed view of the signal's time-varying behavior over different time scales. It is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where signals often exhibit non-stationary behavior over different time scales.




#### 4.2a Beamforming Techniques

Beamforming is a signal processing technique used in acoustical oceanography to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of a received signal. It involves combining the signals received by multiple sensors to form a directional beam that can be steered to focus on a particular direction. This section will discuss the various beamforming techniques used in acoustical oceanography.

##### Delay and Sum Beamforming

Delay and Sum Beamforming (DSB) is a time domain approach to beamforming. It is simple to implement, but it may poorly estimate the direction of arrival (DOA). The solution to this is a frequency domain approach. The Fourier transform transforms the signal from the time domain to the frequency domain. This converts the time delay between adjacent sensors into a phase shift. Thus, the array output vector at any time "t" can be denoted as $\boldsymbol x(t) = x_1(t)\begin{bmatrix} 1 & e^{-j\omega\Delta t} & \cdots & e^{-j\omega(M-1)\Delta t} \end{bmatrix}^T$, where $x_1(t)$ stands for the signal received by the first sensor.

Frequency domain beamforming algorithms use the spatial covariance matrix, represented by $\boldsymbol R=E\{ \boldsymbol x(t) \boldsymbol x^T(t)\}$. This "M" by "M" matrix carries the spatial and spectral information of the incoming signals. Assuming zero-mean Gaussian white noise, the basic model of the spatial covariance matrix is given by

$$
\boldsymbol R = \boldsymbol V \boldsymbol S \boldsymbol V^H + \sigma^2 \boldsymbol I \ \ (4)
$$

where $\sigma^2$ is the variance of the white noise, $\boldsymbol I$ is the identity matrix and $\boldsymbol V$ is the array manifold vector $\boldsymbol V = \begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol v_1 & \cdots & \boldsymbol v_k \end{bmatrix}^T$ with $\boldsymbol v_i = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & e^{-j\omega\Delta t_i} & \cdots & e^{-j\omega(M-1)\Delta t_i} \end{bmatrix}^T$. This model is of central importance in frequency domain beamforming algorithms.

##### Conventional (Bartlett) Beamformer

The Bartlett beamformer is a natural extension of conventional spectral analysis (spectrogram) to the sensor array. Its spectral power is represented by

$$
\hat{P}_{Bartlett}(\theta)=\boldsymbol v^H \boldsymbol R \boldsymbol v \ \ (5)
$$

The Bartlett beamformer is particularly useful in situations where the signal is stationary over the time interval of the beamformer. However, if the signal is non-stationary, the Bartlett beamformer may not perform as well.

In the next section, we will discuss other beamforming techniques, including the minimum variance beamformer and the maximum likelihood beamformer.

#### 4.2b Beamforming Algorithms

Beamforming algorithms are used to process the received signals from multiple sensors to form a directional beam. These algorithms are based on the principles of signal processing and statistics. They are designed to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the incoming signals and to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of the received signals.

##### Minimum Variance Beamforming

The Minimum Variance Beamforming (MVB) algorithm is a frequency domain beamforming algorithm that aims to minimize the variance of the received signals. It is based on the assumption that the incoming signals are zero-mean Gaussian white noise. The MVB algorithm uses the spatial covariance matrix $\boldsymbol R$ to estimate the direction of arrival of the incoming signals. The MVB beamformer is given by

$$
\boldsymbol w_{MVB} = \boldsymbol R^{-1} \boldsymbol v \ \ (6)
$$

where $\boldsymbol w_{MVB}$ is the beamforming vector, $\boldsymbol R^{-1}$ is the inverse of the spatial covariance matrix, and $\boldsymbol v$ is the array manifold vector.

##### Maximum Likelihood Beamforming

The Maximum Likelihood Beamforming (MLB) algorithm is another frequency domain beamforming algorithm that aims to maximize the likelihood of the received signals. It is based on the assumption that the incoming signals are zero-mean Gaussian white noise. The MLB algorithm uses the spatial covariance matrix $\boldsymbol R$ to estimate the direction of arrival of the incoming signals. The MLB beamformer is given by

$$
\boldsymbol w_{MLB} = \boldsymbol R^{-1} \boldsymbol v \ \ (7)
$$

where $\boldsymbol w_{MLB}$ is the beamforming vector, $\boldsymbol R^{-1}$ is the inverse of the spatial covariance matrix, and $\boldsymbol v$ is the array manifold vector.

##### Conventional (Bartlett) Beamforming

The Conventional (Bartlett) Beamforming algorithm is a time domain beamforming algorithm that is a natural extension of conventional spectral analysis (spectrogram) to the sensor array. Its spectral power is represented by

$$
\hat{P}_{Bartlett}(\theta)=\boldsymbol v^H \boldsymbol R \boldsymbol v \ \ (8)
$$

where $\boldsymbol v$ is the array manifold vector. The Bartlett beamformer is particularly useful in situations where the signal is stationary over the time interval of the beamformer.

##### Delay and Sum Beamforming

The Delay and Sum Beamforming (DSB) algorithm is a time domain beamforming algorithm that is simple to implement, but it may poorly estimate the direction of arrival (DOA). The solution to this is a frequency domain approach. The Fourier transform transforms the signal from the time domain to the frequency domain. This converts the time delay between adjacent sensors into a phase shift. Thus, the array output vector at any time "t" can be denoted as $\boldsymbol x(t) = x_1(t)\begin{bmatrix} 1 & e^{-j\omega\Delta t} & \cdots & e^{-j\omega(M-1)\Delta t} \end{bmatrix}^T$, where $x_1(t)$ stands for the signal received by the first sensor.

#### 4.2c Beamforming Applications

Beamforming techniques have a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. They are used to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals, estimate the direction of arrival of incoming signals, and to study the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

##### Underwater Acoustics

In underwater acoustics, beamforming is used to study the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The Minimum Variance Beamforming (MVB) and Maximum Likelihood Beamforming (MLB) algorithms are particularly useful in this context. These algorithms allow us to estimate the direction of arrival of sound waves and to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals. This is crucial for understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean and for predicting their propagation.

##### Sonar Systems

Beamforming is also used in sonar systems. Sonar systems use sound waves to detect and locate objects underwater. Beamforming allows us to steer the direction of the sonar beam, which is crucial for detecting and localizing objects. The Conventional (Bartlett) Beamforming algorithm is particularly useful in this context. It is a time domain beamforming algorithm that is a natural extension of conventional spectral analysis (spectrogram) to the sensor array. Its spectral power is represented by

$$
\hat{P}_{Bartlett}(\theta)=\boldsymbol v^H \boldsymbol R \boldsymbol v \ \ (8)
$$

where $\boldsymbol v$ is the array manifold vector. This equation allows us to estimate the spectral power of the received signals, which is crucial for detecting and localizing objects.

##### Underwater Imaging

Beamforming is also used in underwater imaging. Underwater imaging uses sound waves to create images of the underwater environment. Beamforming allows us to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals, which is crucial for creating high-quality images. The Minimum Variance Beamforming (MVB) and Maximum Likelihood Beamforming (MLB) algorithms are particularly useful in this context. These algorithms allow us to estimate the direction of arrival of sound waves and to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals.

In conclusion, beamforming techniques play a crucial role in acoustical oceanography. They allow us to study the propagation of sound waves in the ocean, detect and localize objects underwater, and create high-quality images of the underwater environment.




#### 4.2b Array Processing Algorithms

Array processing algorithms are a set of mathematical techniques used to process signals received by an array of sensors. These algorithms are designed to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of the received signals, improve the accuracy of direction of arrival estimation, and reduce interference from other sources. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used array processing algorithms in acoustical oceanography.

##### Least-Squares Beamforming

Least-Squares Beamforming (LSB) is a frequency domain approach to beamforming. It is based on the least-squares method, which minimizes the sum of the squares of the residuals. The LSB algorithm uses the spatial covariance matrix, represented by $\boldsymbol R$, to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the incoming signals. The DOA is then used to steer the beam in the desired direction.

The LSB algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol v} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol v} \| \boldsymbol R - \boldsymbol v \boldsymbol v^H \|_F^2 \ \ (5)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol v}$ is the estimated direction of arrival vector, and $\| \cdot \|_F$ denotes the Frobenius norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the eigenvector corresponding to the largest eigenvalue of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$.

##### MUSIC Beamforming

MUSIC (MUltiple SIgnal Classification) Beamforming is another frequency domain approach to beamforming. It is based on the eigenvalue decomposition of the spatial covariance matrix. The MUSIC algorithm uses the eigenvectors of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$ to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the incoming signals. The DOA is then used to steer the beam in the desired direction.

The MUSIC algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol v} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol v} \| \boldsymbol R - \boldsymbol v \boldsymbol v^H \|_F^2 \ \ (6)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol v}$ is the estimated direction of arrival vector, and $\| \cdot \|_F$ denotes the Frobenius norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the eigenvector corresponding to the largest eigenvalue of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$.

##### ESPRIT Beamforming

ESPRIT (Estimation of Signal Parameters via Rotational Invariance Techniques) Beamforming is a frequency domain approach to beamforming. It is based on the concept of rotational invariance, which allows for the estimation of the direction of arrival (DOA) of the incoming signals without the need for a priori knowledge of the signal subspace.

The ESPRIT algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol v} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol v} \| \boldsymbol R - \boldsymbol v \boldsymbol v^H \|_F^2 \ \ (7)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol v}$ is the estimated direction of arrival vector, and $\| \cdot \|_F$ denotes the Frobenius norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the eigenvector corresponding to the largest eigenvalue of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$.

##### Subspace Beamforming

Subspace Beamforming is a frequency domain approach to beamforming. It is based on the concept of subspace, which allows for the estimation of the direction of arrival (DOA) of the incoming signals without the need for a priori knowledge of the signal subspace.

The Subspace Beamforming algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol v} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol v} \| \boldsymbol R - \boldsymbol v \boldsymbol v^H \|_F^2 \ \ (8)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol v}$ is the estimated direction of arrival vector, and $\| \cdot \|_F$ denotes the Frobenius norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the eigenvector corresponding to the largest eigenvalue of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$.

##### MVDR Beamforming

MVDR (Minimum Variance Distortionless Response) Beamforming is a frequency domain approach to beamforming. It is based on the concept of minimum variance, which minimizes the variance of the received signal while maintaining a constant response to the desired signal.

The MVDR Beamforming algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol v} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol v} \| \boldsymbol R - \boldsymbol v \boldsymbol v^H \|_F^2 \ \ (9)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol v}$ is the estimated direction of arrival vector, and $\| \cdot \|_F$ denotes the Frobenius norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the eigenvector corresponding to the largest eigenvalue of the matrix $\boldsymbol R^H \boldsymbol R$.

##### Conclusion

In this section, we have discussed some of the most commonly used array processing algorithms in acoustical oceanography. These algorithms are essential tools for enhancing the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals, improving the accuracy of direction of arrival estimation, and reducing interference from other sources. Each of these algorithms has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of algorithm depends on the specific requirements of the application.




#### 4.2c Adaptive Beamforming

Adaptive Beamforming (ABF) is a technique used in array processing to adjust the beamforming weights in real-time based on the changing characteristics of the received signals. This is particularly useful in environments where the signal characteristics, such as the direction of arrival or the signal-to-noise ratio, vary over time.

##### Least-Squares Adaptive Beamforming

Least-Squares Adaptive Beamforming (LSAB) is a time domain approach to adaptive beamforming. It is based on the least-squares method, similar to the Least-Squares Beamforming (LSB) algorithm. However, in LSAB, the beamforming weights are updated in real-time based on the current signal characteristics.

The LSAB algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol w} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol w} \| \boldsymbol y - \boldsymbol w^T \boldsymbol x \|_2^2 \ \ (7)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol w}$ is the estimated beamforming weight vector, $\boldsymbol y$ is the received signal vector, and $\boldsymbol x$ is the received signal matrix. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the least-squares estimate of the beamforming weight vector.

##### Recursive Least-Squares Adaptive Beamforming

Recursive Least-Squares Adaptive Beamforming (RLSAB) is a variation of the LSAB algorithm that allows for more efficient updating of the beamforming weights. In RLSAB, the beamforming weights are updated recursively based on the current signal characteristics.

The RLSAB algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol w} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol w} \| \boldsymbol y - \boldsymbol w^T \boldsymbol x \|_2^2 + \lambda \| \boldsymbol w \|_2^2 \ \ (8)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol w}$ is the estimated beamforming weight vector, $\boldsymbol y$ is the received signal vector, $\boldsymbol x$ is the received signal matrix, and $\lambda$ is a regularization parameter that controls the trade-off between the signal fit and the weight norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the recursive least-squares estimate of the beamforming weight vector.

##### MUSIC Adaptive Beamforming

MUSIC Adaptive Beamforming (MUSICAB) is another frequency domain approach to adaptive beamforming. It is based on the MUSIC algorithm, similar to the MUSIC Beamforming (MUSIC) algorithm. However, in MUSICAB, the beamforming weights are updated in real-time based on the current signal characteristics.

The MUSICAB algorithm can be represented as follows:

$$
\hat{\boldsymbol w} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol w} \| \boldsymbol y - \boldsymbol w^T \boldsymbol x \|_2^2 + \lambda \| \boldsymbol w \|_2^2 \ \ (9)
$$

where $\hat{\boldsymbol w}$ is the estimated beamforming weight vector, $\boldsymbol y$ is the received signal vector, $\boldsymbol x$ is the received signal matrix, and $\lambda$ is a regularization parameter that controls the trade-off between the signal fit and the weight norm. The solution to this optimization problem is given by the recursive least-squares estimate of the beamforming weight vector.




### Subsection: 4.3a Time Difference of Arrival (TDOA)

Time Difference of Arrival (TDOA) is a method used in acoustical oceanography to determine the location of a sound source. It is based on the principle that the time at which a sound wave arrives at a receiver is directly related to the distance between the receiver and the source.

#### 4.3a.1 Principle of TDOA

The principle of TDOA is based on the fact that the time at which a sound wave arrives at a receiver is directly related to the distance between the receiver and the source. This is due to the speed of sound in water, which is a constant value. The time difference of arrival (TDOA) is the difference in arrival time between two or more receivers.

The TDOA can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
TDOA = T_{receiver2} - T_{receiver1} \ \ (9)
$$

where $T_{receiver2}$ is the time at which the sound wave arrives at the second receiver, and $T_{receiver1}$ is the time at which the sound wave arrives at the first receiver.

#### 4.3a.2 TDOA and Source Localization

TDOA is a powerful tool for source localization in acoustical oceanography. By using multiple receivers, the TDOA can be used to determine the location of a sound source. This is because the TDOA is directly related to the distance between the receivers and the source.

The TDOA can be used in conjunction with other methods, such as beamforming, to improve the accuracy of source localization. By combining the TDOA with other methods, it is possible to accurately localize sound sources in the ocean.

#### 4.3a.3 Challenges and Future Directions

Despite its potential, there are several challenges that need to be addressed in order to fully realize the potential of TDOA in acoustical oceanography. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference, which can affect the accuracy of the TDOA.

In the future, advancements in signal processing techniques and technology will likely improve the accuracy and reliability of TDOA. Additionally, the integration of TDOA with other methods, such as beamforming, will continue to improve the accuracy of source localization in acoustical oceanography.




### Subsection: 4.3b Angle of Arrival (AOA)

Angle of Arrival (AOA) is another method used in acoustical oceanography to determine the location of a sound source. It is based on the principle that the direction of arrival of a sound wave at a receiver is directly related to the direction of the source.

#### 4.3b.1 Principle of AOA

The principle of AOA is based on the fact that the direction of arrival of a sound wave at a receiver is directly related to the direction of the source. This is due to the propagation of sound waves in water, which is a directional phenomenon. The AOA is the angle between the direction of arrival of the sound wave and a reference direction.

The AOA can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
AOA = \arctan\left(\frac{Y_{receiver2} - Y_{receiver1}}{X_{receiver2} - X_{receiver1}}\right) \ \ (10)
$$

where $X_{receiver2}$ and $Y_{receiver2}$ are the coordinates of the second receiver, and $X_{receiver1}$ and $Y_{receiver1}$ are the coordinates of the first receiver.

#### 4.3b.2 AOA and Source Localization

AOA is a powerful tool for source localization in acoustical oceanography. By using multiple receivers, the AOA can be used to determine the direction of a sound source. This is because the AOA is directly related to the direction of the source.

The AOA can be used in conjunction with other methods, such as TDOA, to improve the accuracy of source localization. By combining the AOA with other methods, it is possible to accurately localize sound sources in the ocean.

#### 4.3b.3 Challenges and Future Directions

Despite its potential, there are several challenges that need to be addressed in order to fully realize the potential of AOA in acoustical oceanography. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference, which can affect the accuracy of the AOA.

In the future, advancements in signal processing techniques and technology will likely improve the accuracy and reliability of AOA. Additionally, the integration of AOA with other methods, such as TDOA, will provide a more comprehensive approach to source localization in acoustical oceanography.




### Subsection: 4.3c Amplitude-based Methods

Amplitude-based methods are another class of techniques used in acoustical oceanography for source localization. These methods are based on the principle that the amplitude of a sound wave at a receiver is directly related to the distance between the source and the receiver.

#### 4.3c.1 Principle of Amplitude-based Methods

The principle of amplitude-based methods is based on the fact that the amplitude of a sound wave at a receiver is directly related to the distance between the source and the receiver. This is due to the inverse square law of sound propagation in water. The amplitude of a sound wave decreases with the square of the distance from the source.

The amplitude of a sound wave at a receiver can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
A_{receiver} = \frac{A_{source}}{r^2} \ \ (11)
$$

where $A_{source}$ is the amplitude of the sound wave at the source, and $r$ is the distance between the source and the receiver.

#### 4.3c.2 Amplitude-based Methods and Source Localization

Amplitude-based methods are a powerful tool for source localization in acoustical oceanography. By using multiple receivers, the amplitude of a sound source can be determined. This information can then be used to localize the source.

The amplitude of a sound source can be used in conjunction with other methods, such as TDOA and AOA, to improve the accuracy of source localization. By combining the amplitude with other methods, it is possible to accurately localize sound sources in the ocean.

#### 4.3c.3 Challenges and Future Directions

Despite their potential, there are several challenges that need to be addressed in order to fully realize the potential of amplitude-based methods in acoustical oceanography. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference, which can affect the accuracy of the amplitude measurements.

In the future, advancements in signal processing techniques and technology will likely improve the accuracy and reliability of amplitude-based methods. Additionally, the integration of amplitude-based methods with other techniques, such as AOA and TDOA, will provide a more comprehensive approach to source localization in the ocean.




### Section: 4.4 Signal Detection

Signal detection is a crucial aspect of acoustical oceanography, as it allows us to identify and locate sound sources in the ocean. In this section, we will explore the various methods and techniques used for signal detection in the ocean.

#### 4.4a Matched Filter Detection

Matched filter detection is a common method used in signal detection. It is based on the principle of correlation, which is used to detect a signal in noise. The matched filter is designed to maximize the signal-to-noise ratio of a received signal, making it easier to detect the signal.

The matched filter works by correlating the received signal with a known template signal. The template signal is a representation of the expected signal, and it is used to filter out noise and interference. The output of the matched filter is then compared to a threshold to determine if a signal is present.

The matched filter can be represented mathematically as follows:

$$
y(t) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(\tau)h(t-\tau)d\tau \ \ (12)
$$

where $x(t)$ is the received signal, $h(t)$ is the template signal, and $y(t)$ is the output of the matched filter.

The matched filter is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, as it allows us to detect signals in the presence of noise and interference. However, it is important to note that the matched filter is only effective if the received signal is similar to the template signal. If there are significant differences between the two, the matched filter may not be able to detect the signal.

#### 4.4b Energy Detection

Energy detection is another common method used in signal detection. It is based on the principle of energy, which is the total power of a signal. The energy detector compares the energy of the received signal to a threshold to determine if a signal is present.

The energy of a signal can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
E = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} |x(t)|^2 dt \ \ (13)
$$

where $x(t)$ is the received signal.

Energy detection is a simple and effective method for detecting signals in the ocean. However, it is important to note that it is not as robust as matched filter detection, as it is more susceptible to noise and interference.

#### 4.4c Applications of Signal Detection

Signal detection has a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. It is used for source localization, as discussed in the previous section. It is also used for detecting and tracking marine mammals, as well as for monitoring underwater noise pollution.

In addition, signal detection is used in oceanographic research for studying the behavior of marine animals and their interactions with the environment. It is also used in oceanographic mapping and surveying for creating accurate maps of the ocean floor and identifying underwater features.

Overall, signal detection plays a crucial role in understanding and exploring the depths of sound in the ocean. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more applications of signal detection in acoustical oceanography.
